Shelter Construction (22) -

Building a Log Cabin
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
### Building a Log Cabin

1. Select logs: Choose timber that is plentiful and uniform in diameter.
2. Cut logs: Cut the logs to within a few inches of the required lengths for construction.
3. Lay bed logs: Start with the heaviest logs...
### Building a Log Cabin

1. Select logs: Choose timber that is plentiful and uniform in diameter.
2. Cut logs: Cut the logs to within a few inches of the required lengths for construction.
3. Lay bed logs: Start with the heaviest logs as the base (bed logs), ensuring they are laid square.
4. Scarf joints: Where the end logs lie across the back and front logs, halve or scarf the sites for the log to ensure a secure fit.
5. Carve logs into each other: Continue with the remainder of the construction by carving each subsequent log into the previous one to form a tight fit.
6. Fill chinks: Fill any gaps (chinks) between the logs with clay to provide insulation and weather protection.
log cabin, construction, shelter, building materials
Constructing Rammed Earth Walls Using Forms
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
### Constructing Rammed Earth Walls Using Forms

1. Prepare forms: Create wooden forms that are no more than 2' or 3' [60 or 90 cm] high and 6' to 8' [1.8 to 2.4 m] long.
2. Secure forms: Use bolts to hold the forms together; when tightened,...
### Constructing Rammed Earth Walls Using Forms

1. Prepare forms: Create wooden forms that are no more than 2' or 3' [60 or 90 cm] high and 6' to 8' [1.8 to 2.4 m] long.
2. Secure forms: Use bolts to hold the forms together; when tightened, they should clamp the form to the wall.
3. Fill with earth: Shovel in 3" or 4" [7.5 or 10 cm] of soil into the form.
4. Ram the earth: Use a rammer made from a hardwood base (about 4" x 4" x 10" [10 x 10 x 25 cm]) and a handle made from a 5' [1.5 m] length of gaspipe. Ram until the earth rings — this is a distinct sound different from the soft thud thud of initial ramming.
5. Repeat: Once the layer is ringing throughout its length, add another 3" or 4" [7.5 or 10 cm] of soil and repeat the ramming process.
6. Thickness requirements: Ensure that the final wall thickness is at least 9" to 10" [22.5 to 25 cm] for an 8' [2.4 m] wall, or 12" to 15" [30 to 37.5 cm] if a top structure or greater height is required.
7. Protect from weather: Provide a wide overhang to the eave, plaster with cement or lime mortar, or apply a thick cement-sand mixture (one-to-two proportion) as a protective layer.
8. Work rate: One man can fill and ram about 9 to 12 cubic feet [0.25 to 0.34 cubic meters] in a day.
rammed earth, forms, construction, shelter, building materials
Using Natural Materials for Lashing in Bushcraft
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
### Using Natural Materials for Lashing in Bushcraft

When manufactured lashing materials are unavailable, natural materials can be used effectively:

Vines: Use ground or tree vines that have length, strength, and pliability. Test them by...
### Using Natural Materials for Lashing in Bushcraft

When manufactured lashing materials are unavailable, natural materials can be used effectively:

Vines: Use ground or tree vines that have length, strength, and pliability. Test them by exerting a steady pull — if they break under 60 or 80 lbs [25 or 35 kg], they are too weak. To test for pliability, tie a thumb knot in the vine and gently pull it tight. If the vine snaps or cuts itself, discard it.

Palm leaves: The outer skin of long palm leaves can be used as lashing material. Harvest by nicking the hard outer shell with a cut about ¼" [6 mm] wide and 1/8" [3 mm] deep. Start splitting the outer cane, but bend the thick portion away from the thin to prevent it from running off.

Bark: The inner bark of many shrubs and trees (alive or dead) can also be used for lashing. Strip down to the required thickness, being careful to avoid weak spots.
natural materials, lashing, bushcraft, shelter, building materials
Build a Thatched Hut Using Local Materials
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Use locally available materials to construct a comfortable, weatherproof hut.
2. Design the hut to provide protection against anticipated weather conditions.
3. Collect suitable materials from the surrounding area; organization...
Procedure:

1. Use locally available materials to construct a comfortable, weatherproof hut.
2. Design the hut to provide protection against anticipated weather conditions.
3. Collect suitable materials from the surrounding area; organization may be required for collection.
4. Good teamwork is necessary for construction and thatching of the structure.
5. Use natural materials such as palm leaves or eucalypt branches for thatching.
6. For long-term durability, consider using rammed earth for walls, which can last hundreds of years with proper construction.
7. Thatched huts can have a service life of 4 to 6 years without maintenance, and up to 20 years with regular upkeep such as renewing lashings and repairing the ridge thatch.
8. The final hut provides long-term protection and shelter through careful planning and execution.
thatched hut, local materials, shelter, thatching, rammed earth, construction
Add Bracing to Huts for Stability
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Purpose: To reinforce long huts or lean-to structures and make them storm-proof when there is no strong support (e.g., a tree).

Steps:
1. Identify Need for Bracing: Use diagonal bracings if the structure lacks natural support, such as...
Purpose: To reinforce long huts or lean-to structures and make them storm-proof when there is no strong support (e.g., a tree).

Steps:
1. Identify Need for Bracing: Use diagonal bracings if the structure lacks natural support, such as in open areas.
2. Install Diagonal Braces: Lash diagonal braces from the ground at one end of the hut to the ridge or crown at the opposite end. These should be securely fastened with lashing material (e.g., rope or sinew).
3. Ensure Structural Integrity:
- The bracings help prevent sagging or inward bending of main poles.
- They distribute weight evenly across the structure, making it more resistant to wind and weather.

Notes:
• This method is particularly useful in storm-prone areas.
• Bracing can be made from available materials (e.g., branches, poles) without requiring purchased items.
• The lashing must be tight enough to withstand strong winds.
bracing for huts, storm-proof shelter, diagonal bracings, lashing material, structural reinforcement
Building a Small Woodshed for Emergency Use
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure for building a small woodshed:

1. Determine dimensions: The ground area of the woodshed should be at least 3' by 4' [0.9 m by 1.2 m], with about 3' [0.9 m] height at the front.
2. Position strategically: Place the woodshed to...
Procedure for building a small woodshed:

1. Determine dimensions: The ground area of the woodshed should be at least 3' by 4' [0.9 m by 1.2 m], with about 3' [0.9 m] height at the front.
2. Position strategically: Place the woodshed to windward of your fireplace so that windblown sparks do not fall on dry bark or other tinder.
3. Purpose: The woodshed ensures a supply of dry kindling and wood after heavy rain, preparing you for spells of wet weather.
woodshed construction, fire protection, emergency shelter
Building a Swinging Shelter
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Forked pole (4" to 5" [10 to 12.5 cm] thick, 8' [2.4 m] long)
• Side branch (4' to 5' [1.2 to 1.5 m] below the fork)
• Rope or strong vine loop
• Tree trunk for securing the shelter
• Stakes (3' [90 cm], about 2" [5 cm]...
Materials Required:
• Forked pole (4" to 5" [10 to 12.5 cm] thick, 8' [2.4 m] long)
• Side branch (4' to 5' [1.2 to 1.5 m] below the fork)
• Rope or strong vine loop
• Tree trunk for securing the shelter
• Stakes (3' [90 cm], about 2" [5 cm] thick)
• Thatching battens (about 1" [2.5 cm] thick, 8' [2.4 m] long)
• Grass, fern palms, or reeds for thatching
• Cross-bars for bed support

Steps:
1. Select Forked Pole: Use a forked pole at least 4" to 5" [10 to 12.5 cm] thick and 8' [2.4 m] long, with a side branch coming off at right angles to the fork and 4' to 5' [1.2 to 1.5 m] below it.
2. Secure Side Branch: To the side branch, secure a rope or very strong vine loop. Pass this around a tree trunk and bind it very securely back on to the side branch.
3. Position Long Arm of Pole: The long arm of the pole should be horizontal and 6' to 7' [1.8 to 2.1 m] above the ground.
4. Lash Stakes to Side of Pole: Lash three 3' [90 cm] stakes, each about 2" [5 cm] thick, to each side of the pole. They should slope down at an angle of about 45° and can be held outwards by lashing braces across.
5. Lash Thatching Battens: Lengthways to these poles, lash thatching battens, each about 1" [2.5 cm] thick and 8' [2.4 m] long. These should be 6" [15 cm] apart.
6. Thatch with Natural Materials: Thatch the battens with grass, fern palms, or reeds (branches and tree leaves are useless).
7. Construct Bed Support: The bed is suspended from the centre pole by ropes or vines to the two long sides, which are held apart by lashing two cross-bars at head and foot.
8. Make Up Bed: The bed is then made up like a camp bed.
9. Swing Shelter for Sun/Weather Protection: This shelter can be swung round the tree trunk to take advantage of sun or shade or get better protection from the weather.
swinging shelter,bushcraft,construction,tree trunk,thatching
Constructing a Single Rope Ladder
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Hardwood chocks (1½" to 2" [4 to 5 cm] thick)
• Rope
• ½" [12 mm] thick hardwood pegs (3" [7.5 cm] long) if using non-braided rope
• Cotton rag or finely teased bark fibre for wick (if needed)

Steps:
1. Cut Chocks:...
Materials Required:
• Hardwood chocks (1½" to 2" [4 to 5 cm] thick)
• Rope
• ½" [12 mm] thick hardwood pegs (3" [7.5 cm] long) if using non-braided rope
• Cotton rag or finely teased bark fibre for wick (if needed)

Steps:
1. Cut Chocks: Cut as many hardwood chocks, 1½" to 2" [4 to 5 cm] thick, as required for your ladder.
2. Spacing: Place the chocks every 15" to 18" [38 to 45 cm] apart along the rope.
3. Size of Chocks: The chocks should be about 4" [10 cm] across and can be cut from either square or round timber.
4. Bore Holes: Bore a hole through the centre of each chock. This hole should not be more than 1/8" [3 mm] larger than the diameter of the rope.
5. Thread Rope Through Chocks: Thread the rope through the holes in the chocks.
6. Insert Peg (if using non-braided rope): Starting at one end, open the strand of the rope and slip in a ½" [12 mm] thick hardwood peg about 3" [7.5 cm] long.
7. Bind Rope Below Peg: Bind the rope below the peg.
8. Slide Chock Down: Slide the chock down, and measure off the distance to the next step.
9. Optional Binding Above Chock: If desired, bind above the chock to prevent the feet pulling it up when climbing.
10. For Braided Cotton Rope: If using braided cotton rope, omit the peg and put a strong binding below each chock.
rope ladder,bushcraft,construction,chocks,hardwood,ropes

2. Ensure coverage: The crown should fully cover the stitching on the ridge to...
Crown ridge thatch is a third method of securing the ridge.

1. Create a crown: Make a "crown" and overlap it over the ends of the top layer of thatching.
2. Ensure coverage: The crown should fully cover the stitching on the ridge to prevent leaks.
crown ridge thatch, roof protection, shelter construction
Determine Pole Length and Diameter for Sloped Structures
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Purpose: To calculate the required length and diameter of poles for sloped structures (e.g., roofs or huts) to ensure stability and waterproofing.

Steps:
1. Calculate Slope Angle: Ensure all slopes are at least 45°, though a minimum of...
Purpose: To calculate the required length and diameter of poles for sloped structures (e.g., roofs or huts) to ensure stability and waterproofing.

Steps:
1. Calculate Slope Angle: Ensure all slopes are at least 45°, though a minimum of 40° is acceptable for shedding water.
2. Determine Base Spread and Pole Length: For a 45° slope, the proportion between base spread and pole length is 4:3 (base to height). Example:
- If the base spread is 20' [6 m], then the pole length should be 15' [4.5 m].
3. Account for Wall Height: Allow a wall height of 3' to 4' [0.9 to 1.2 m] if needed.
4. Calculate Diameter of Poles:
- For poles up to 10' [3 m], the minimum diameter at the butt (excluding bark) should be 1" [2.5 cm] per 4' to 5' [1.2 to 1.5 m] of length.
- Example: A pole that is 10' [3 m] long requires a minimum diameter of 2½" [6.5 cm].
- For poles up to 20' [6 m], the minimum diameter at the butt should be 5" [12.5 cm].

Notes:
• This calculation assumes no purchased materials are used.
• These measurements ensure that the structure is stable and weather-proof for extended periods.
pole length calculation, slope angle, diameter of poles, shelter construction, waterproofing
Guttering Installation for Roofs
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Guttering is necessary under certain conditions to manage water flow on the roof.

1. Use wide bark sheets: Invert wide sheets of bark so that they lie with the hollow side in the valley for efficient guttering.
2. Alternative materials:...
Guttering is necessary under certain conditions to manage water flow on the roof.

1. Use wide bark sheets: Invert wide sheets of bark so that they lie with the hollow side in the valley for efficient guttering.
2. Alternative materials: Use hollowed-out palm trunks or extra-wide leaves from plicate palms laid to overlap each other.
3. Careful installation: Take considerable care during installation to ensure a watertight roof.
guttering, roof construction, water management
How to Flash a Tree or Pole Through a Thatch Roof
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Identify the tree or pole that is projecting through the roof thatch.
2. Spin up a length of thin rope from grass or other soft fibrous material (refer to Bushcraft 'Ropemaking' for detailed instructions on ropemaking).
3. Bind...
Procedure:

1. Identify the tree or pole that is projecting through the roof thatch.
2. Spin up a length of thin rope from grass or other soft fibrous material (refer to Bushcraft 'Ropemaking' for detailed instructions on ropemaking).
3. Bind the thatching material tightly around the tree or pole, ensuring it is secure and covers the area where the tree or pole projects through the roof.
4. Continue the binding an inch or two above the top of the thatching material to ensure proper coverage and protection from rain.
5. Ensure the binding is tight and secure to prevent water from seeping through.

Details:
• The purpose of flashing is to direct rainwater away from the roof structure and into the thatch, where it can be naturally led off the roof.
• Use soft fibrous materials for rope-making to avoid damaging the tree or pole.
flashing, thatching, rope making, shelter construction, rainwater management
Ridge Thatching Technique
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Ridge thatching is essential for complete protection of the roof.

1. Cover the stitching: Ensure the topmost row of thatching completely covers the stitching on the ridge pole or a false ridge pole.
2. Alternative method: Allow the...
Ridge thatching is essential for complete protection of the roof.

1. Cover the stitching: Ensure the topmost row of thatching completely covers the stitching on the ridge pole or a false ridge pole.
2. Alternative method: Allow the ridge thatch to stand up from the ridge and bind it tightly for an efficient watershed.
3. Efficiency for hut types: This method is most efficient for pyramidal and circular huts.
ridge thatching, roof protection, shelter construction
Sewn Batten Thatch Application
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Sewn batten thatch is a neat and efficient method for certain materials.

1. Prepare the leaves: Bend long, broad-leaved materials over sticks on the ground.
2. Use sewing material: Use a thin sliver of split cane or other suitable...
Sewn batten thatch is a neat and efficient method for certain materials.

1. Prepare the leaves: Bend long, broad-leaved materials over sticks on the ground.
2. Use sewing material: Use a thin sliver of split cane or other suitable material to sew the two sections of the leaves together.
3. Secure to framework: Tie the sticks to the framework as for split stalk thatching.
4. Material considerations: If using green material, ensure it will not curl as it dries out. Dead material is generally best.
sewn batten thatch, broad-leaved materials, shelter construction
Sewn Ridge Thatching Technique
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Sewn ridge thatching is a method for securing long materials to the ridge.

1. Use heavy poles: For very long material, use two heavy poles slung on slings to hold the outside edges of the ridge thatch material down.
2. Tie battens: Tie...
Sewn ridge thatching is a method for securing long materials to the ridge.

1. Use heavy poles: For very long material, use two heavy poles slung on slings to hold the outside edges of the ridge thatch material down.
2. Tie battens: Tie two battens to the top of the topmost layer of thatching and ensure the ends of the ridge thatch material overhang the sewing of the topmost layer.
3. Use three poles: Sew the ridge material on to three poles, with one acting as a false ridge and the other two hanging over the ridge by 12" or 18" [30 or 45 cm] on either side of the centre pole.
4. Lay materials: Lay the ridge thatch material in lengths of from 6' to 12' [1.8 to 3.6 m] and secure them over the actual ridge.
sewn ridge thatching, long materials, shelter construction
Stalk Thatch Application
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Stalk thatch is a quick and efficient method suitable for both plicate and pinnate type palm leaves.

1. Prepare the materials: Ensure you have well-bunched palm leaves with good overlap to create an effective watershed.
2. **Weave the...
Stalk thatch is a quick and efficient method suitable for both plicate and pinnate type palm leaves.

1. Prepare the materials: Ensure you have well-bunched palm leaves with good overlap to create an effective watershed.
2. Weave the stalks: Simply weave the stalks between the thatch battens. The natural bend of the stalks will exert sufficient pressure to hold the leaves securely in position.
3. Check for effectiveness: Ensure the thatching is properly overlapped and secured to prevent leaks.
stalk thatch, palm leaves, thatching methods, shelter construction
Testing Soil Quality for Rammed Earth Construction
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Take a sample of the soil and roll it into a ball about the size of a golf ball.
2. Drop the ball from a height of 1' [30 cm]. If it breaks up, the soil is too dry and needs moisture added before ramming.
3. If the ball does not...
Procedure:

1. Take a sample of the soil and roll it into a ball about the size of a golf ball.
2. Drop the ball from a height of 1' [30 cm]. If it breaks up, the soil is too dry and needs moisture added before ramming.
3. If the ball does not break when dropped from 1' [30 cm], hold it above your head and drop it again from a height of 6' to 7' [1.8 to 2.1 m].
4. If the ball shatters into small fragments, the soil is too moist and must be allowed to dry out before ramming.
5. Use a glass tube that is ten inches long or divided into ten equal parts for further testing:
- Dry some of the earth, crumble it to fine powder, and fill the tube.
- Wash the contents in running water until all clay and silt particles are removed.
- Dry the remaining material and put it back into the tube. The level will indicate the approximate percentage of clayey content in the soil.

Details:
• Ideal soil for rammed earth construction should have between 30% to 70% sand or grit, and between 30% to 70% clay and silt.
• If the soil has too much clay, it will crack; if too little clay or too much sand or organic matter, it will crumble.
soil testing, rammed earth construction, building materials
Woven Thatch Application
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Woven thatch is an alternative method to split stalk thatch and is suitable when time permits and materials are readily available.

1. Lay the fronds: Lay pinnate fronds flat on the ground.
2. Weave the leaves: Lay the leaves from one...
Woven thatch is an alternative method to split stalk thatch and is suitable when time permits and materials are readily available.

1. Lay the fronds: Lay pinnate fronds flat on the ground.
2. Weave the leaves: Lay the leaves from one side over and weave them between the leaves on the other side.
3. Secure to framework: Tie the entire stalk to the framework, ensuring proper overlap as with other thatching methods.
woven thatch, pinnate fronds, shelter construction
Construct a Circular Hut Step by Step
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Fork poles
• Rafters
• Thatching battens
• Wall battens
• Lashing material (e.g., rope or sinew)

Steps to Construct a Circular Hut:
1. Start the Basic Structure: Begin with four men working on the structure. Use...
Materials Required:
• Fork poles
• Rafters
• Thatching battens
• Wall battens
• Lashing material (e.g., rope or sinew)

Steps to Construct a Circular Hut:
1. Start the Basic Structure: Begin with four men working on the structure. Use fork poles lashed together with thatching battens and wall battens. Ensure rafters are in place, and the door fork is swung into position.
2. Set Entrance Ridge and Poles: Position the entrance ridge and entrance poles along with wall poles to form the basic shape of the hut.
3. Lash Thatching Battens: Secure thatching battens to the rafters, ensuring they are strong enough to bear weight (e.g., a man's weight). This is critical for structural integrity.
4. Complete the Door and Begin Thatching: After one-and-a-half hours, the door should be complete and ready for thatching. Start laying thatch on the roof from this point.
5. Lay Roof Thatch: By two-and-a-half hours after starting, three rows of roof thatch should be laid. The hut is 15' [4.5 m] in diameter from wall to wall and can be completed within one hour (or three-and-a-half hours total) after the start.
6. Finalize Construction: Ensure all lashing is secure and that the structure is weather-proof for six to ten years with no nails or purchased materials used.

Notes:
• This hut was constructed by four men in three-and-a-half hours.
• The diameter of the hut from wall to wall is 15' [4.5 m].
• All lashing must be done carefully for structural integrity.
circular hut, shelter construction, thatching battens, lashing material, rafters, fork poles
Construct a Long Hut
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Fork poles
• Intermediate poles
• Ridge pole
• Thatching battens
• Wall pegs
• Lashing material (e.g., rope or sinew)

Steps to Construct a Long Hut:
1. Plan the Structure: The long hut is 60' [18 m] long, 20' [6 m]...
Materials Required:
• Fork poles
• Intermediate poles
• Ridge pole
• Thatching battens
• Wall pegs
• Lashing material (e.g., rope or sinew)

Steps to Construct a Long Hut:
1. Plan the Structure: The long hut is 60' [18 m] long, 20' [6 m] wide, and 16' [4.8 m] high. If the span exceeds 12', intermediate poles are required to support the structure.
2. Prepare the Ridge Pole: Extend the ridge pole to the desired length if needed. If it is in two or more lengths, lash them together securely. Notch the ridge so that it fits snugly into the interlocking forks at the top of the fork poles.
3. Set Fork Poles and Intermediate Poles: Place fork poles with intermediate poles spaced no more than 6' [1.8 m] apart. Lash collar ties to the fork poles for stability.
4. Install Wall Pegs and Thatching Battens: Drive wall pegs into the ground at a convenient height. Attach thatching battens to the rafters, ensuring they are strong enough to bear weight (e.g., a man's weight).
5. Add Ventilation or Light Openings: If desired, include "lift up" sections for ventilation and light.
6. Complete the Structure: The end portion of the long hut is similar to one-half section of the pyramid hut. Continue extending the length as needed by adding more fork poles and ridge segments.

Notes:
• This structure can be built by five men in four days.
• No nails or purchased materials are required.
• The completed hut will be serviceable and weather-proof for six to ten years.
long hut, shelter construction, fork poles, ridge pole, thatching battens, wall pegs, lashing material
Constructing a Rammed Earth Building
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Select earth containing the right proportions of clay and sand or other gritty particles. The soil must be free from organic materials such as grass, roots, and similar matter.
2. Prepare the foundation by setting large stones in...
Procedure:

1. Select earth containing the right proportions of clay and sand or other gritty particles. The soil must be free from organic materials such as grass, roots, and similar matter.
2. Prepare the foundation by setting large stones in clay within the foundation trench. Clay is preferred over concrete for rammed earth buildings because it is impervious to moisture.
3. If using concrete foundations, install a dampcourse to prevent water infiltration.
4. Construct the walls either by erecting forms or by ramming earth into blocks (like large bricks) and laying them in courses.
5. Ensure that the foundation extends 6" to 9" [15 to 22.5 cm] above ground level to protect against surface runoff from heavy rain.

Details:
• Rammed earth buildings are permanent, well-insulated, and low-cost structures.
• The method is speedy and can be done by one person digging and laying 15' to 20' [4.5 to 6 m] of foundation in a day.
rammed earth, shelter construction, building materials, foundation preparation
Preparing Foundations for Rammed Earth Buildings
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Dig a trench 2' [60 cm] wide by 1' [30 cm] deep for the foundation.
2. Line the trench with at least 1" [2.5 cm] of clay to ensure moisture resistance.
3. Place large stones in the clay-lined trench and ram them down to create a...
Procedure:

1. Dig a trench 2' [60 cm] wide by 1' [30 cm] deep for the foundation.
2. Line the trench with at least 1" [2.5 cm] of clay to ensure moisture resistance.
3. Place large stones in the clay-lined trench and ram them down to create a firm bed.
4. Ensure that the foundations extend from 6" to 9" [15 to 22.5 cm] above ground level.

Details:
• This method is cost-effective, as no materials are purchased beyond natural resources.
• The foundation should be constructed in such a way that it can withstand heavy rain without surface runoff reaching the rammed earth wall.
foundation preparation, shelter construction, rammed earth building

Rope Making (18) -

Get a Rope Across a Narrow Deep River
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Stout stick
• Bush-made rope
• Forked tree on the opposite bank

Steps to Get the Rope Across:
1. Fasten a stout stick to the end of the rope, ensuring it is centered on the stick.
2. Select a forked tree on the opposite...
Materials Needed:
• Stout stick
• Bush-made rope
• Forked tree on the opposite bank

Steps to Get the Rope Across:
1. Fasten a stout stick to the end of the rope, ensuring it is centered on the stick.
2. Select a forked tree on the opposite bank and throw the free end of the coiled line with the stick across the river.
3. After multiple casts, test the line with two or three people to ensure it is secure.
4. Fasten the near end of the rope to a convenient anchor.
5. The lightest member of the party should cross the line by hanging onto it and working themselves across, hooking their legs over the rope with feet facing the opposite bank.
6. Once across, they can fasten the rope and complete any necessary tasks on the other side.
cross river, rope across river, survival, bushcraft
Construct a Rope Bridge
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Two strong ropes (thoroughly tested)
• 'A' frames made from light saplings
• Anchors or trees on either side of the river

Steps to Construct the Bridge:
1. Spin and test two strong ropes.
2. Anchor both ropes to...
Materials Needed:
• Two strong ropes (thoroughly tested)
• 'A' frames made from light saplings
• Anchors or trees on either side of the river

Steps to Construct the Bridge:
1. Spin and test two strong ropes.
2. Anchor both ropes to convenient trees or anchors on either side of the river, ensuring they are stretched taut.
3. Make 'A' frames from light saplings. The number required depends on the length of the decking.
4. Hook the first 'A' frame onto the ropes and push it forward with a stick.
5. Drop a straight sapling (footing) into the crotch of the frame.
6. Walk out along this footing, hooking on the next 'A' frame and pushing it to the required distance.
7. Repeat until reaching the far bank.

Safety Notes:
• Do not overload the bridge; one person at a time is a safe rule.
• If available, use natural ropes like Monkey vines, Lianas, or Lawyer vines (Calamus) for added strength and safety.
rope bridge, river crossing, survival, bushcraft
Common Faults in Rope Making for Beginners
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Common Faults in Rope Making for Beginners

1. Uneven feeding: There is a tendency with the beginner to feed unevenly. Thin wispy sections of strand are followed by thick hunky portions. Such feeding is useless. Rope made from such strands...
Common Faults in Rope Making for Beginners

1. Uneven feeding: There is a tendency with the beginner to feed unevenly. Thin wispy sections of strand are followed by thick hunky portions. Such feeding is useless. Rope made from such strands will break with less than one-quarter of the possible strain from the material.
2. Slow and steady approach: The beginner is wise to twist and feed slowly, and to make regular, even strands rather than rush the job and try and make the strands quickly. Speed, with uniformity of twist and thickness, comes only with practice.
3. Feeding speed mastery: In a short time when you have the 'feel' of feeding, you will find you can feed at the rate of from 30' to 60' [9 m to 18 m] a minute.
4. Avoid thick strands: Thick strands do not help. It is useless to try and spin up a rope from strands 1" [2.5 cm] or more in thickness. Such a rope will break with less than half the potential strain of the material.
5. Time-saving misconception: Spinning 'thick' strands does not save time in ropemaking.
beginner faults, uneven feeding, thick strands, strain reduction, speed with practice
Create a Single Rope Ladder With Sticks
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Bush-made rope
• Straight sticks (about 8" [20 cm] long)

Steps to Create the Ladder:
1. Open the lays of the rope.
2. Insert cross sticks into the rope, ensuring each stick is about 8" [20 cm] long and protrudes equally...
Materials Needed:
• Bush-made rope
• Straight sticks (about 8" [20 cm] long)

Steps to Create the Ladder:
1. Open the lays of the rope.
2. Insert cross sticks into the rope, ensuring each stick is about 8" [20 cm] long and protrudes equally on both sides of the rope.
3. Secure the cross sticks to the rope by lashing above and below them.
4. Ensure that the distance between the cross sticks is from 15" to 18" [37.5 cm to 45 cm].

How to Climb the Ladder:
1. Hold the rope with both hands.
2. Bend your knees and draw both feet up together, placing them evenly on the next cross sticks.
3. When the footing is secure, raise your hands and continue climbing in a manner similar to a toy monkey on a string.

Advantages:
• Easy for people who may not be able to climb by ordinary means.
• Useful for ascending or descending cliffs or lookouts.
single rope ladder, sticks, climbing, bushcraft, survival
Determining Correct Strand Thickness and Twist
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Determining Correct Strand Thickness and Twist

1. Strand thickness guidelines: Equal thickness for each of the strands throughout their length, and equal twist are important. The thickness should not be greater than is necessary with the...
Determining Correct Strand Thickness and Twist

1. Strand thickness guidelines: Equal thickness for each of the strands throughout their length, and equal twist are important. The thickness should not be greater than is necessary with the material being used.
- For grass rope: The strand should not be more than ¼" [6 mm] diameter.
- For coarse bark or palm: Not more than 1⁄8" or 3⁄16" [3 mm or 4.5 mm].
- For fine bark, hair or sisal fibre: Not more than 1⁄8" [3 mm].
- For cords: The strand should be no more than 1⁄16" [1.5 mm] in diameter.
2. Fine cord limitations: Fine cords cannot be made from grass, unless the fibres are separated by beating out and 'combing'.
3. Correct twist: The correct amount of twist is when the material is 'hard', that is, the twist is tight.
strand thickness, grass rope, coarse bark, fine cord, twist guidelines, hard twist
Extracting and Preparing Bark Fibres for Rope Making
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Bark fibres in many barks suitable for rope making are close to the innermost layer (the bark next to the sap wood).

When seeking suitable barks of green timber, cut a small section about 3" [7.5 cm] long and 1" [2.5 cm] wide, cutting from...
Bark fibres in many barks suitable for rope making are close to the innermost layer (the bark next to the sap wood).

When seeking suitable barks of green timber, cut a small section about 3" [7.5 cm] long and 1" [2.5 cm] wide, cutting from the wood to the outer skin of the bark.

Peel this specimen and test the different layers. Green bark fibres are generally difficult to spin because of 'gum'—it is better to search for windfallen dead branches and try the inner bark of these, as the gum will have leached out and the fibres separate easily.

Many shrubs have excellent bark fibre. It is advisable to cut the end of a branch and peel off a strip of bark for testing. Thin barks from green shrubs are sometimes difficult to spin into fine cord; in such cases, it is easier to use the lariat plait for small cords.

If using green bark fibre for rope spinning (and time permits), tease and soak the bark strips in water for a day or so. The gum will generally wash out during this process.

After removing from the water, allow the bark strips to partly dry out before shredding and teasing into fibre.
bark fibres,bushcraft,rope making
Extracting and Using Lianas, Vines, and Canes for Rope Making
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Lianas and ground vines are natural ropes found in sub-tropical and tropical scrub and jungle. Many are of great strength and useful for bridging, tree climbing, and other purposes. The smaller ground vines when plaited give great strength and...
Lianas and ground vines are natural ropes found in sub-tropical and tropical scrub and jungle. Many are of great strength and useful for bridging, tree climbing, and other purposes. The smaller ground vines when plaited give great strength and flexibility.

Canes and stalks of palms provide excellent material if used properly. Only the outer skin is tough and strong, and this skin will split off easily if you bend the main stalk away from the skin. This principle also applies to the splitting of lawyer cane (calamus), all the palm leaf stalks, and all green material.

If the split starts to run off, you must bend the material away from the thin side, and then it will gradually gain in size and come back to an even thickness with the other split side.
lianas,vines,canes,bushcraft,rope making
Feeding Technique for Uniform Strand Thickness
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Feeding Technique for Uniform Strand Thickness

1. Position the material: If the feeder is holding material under his left arm, his light hand is engaged in continuously pulling material forward to his left hand which feeds it into the...
Feeding Technique for Uniform Strand Thickness

1. Position the material: If the feeder is holding material under his left arm, his light hand is engaged in continuously pulling material forward to his left hand which feeds it into the turning strand.
2. Control feeding speed: These actions done together as the feeder walks backwards govern the thickness of the strands.
3. Feel for twist: *His left hand, lightly closed over the loose turning material, must 'feel' the fibres 'biting' or twisting together.*
4. Adjust for evenness: When the free end of the turning strand, which is against the loose material under his arm, takes in too thick a tuft of the material he closes his left hand, and so arrests the twist of the material between his left hand and his bundle.
5. Correct overfull 'bite': This allows him to tease out the overfull 'bite' with his right hand, and so he maintains a uniform thickness of the spinning strand.
6. Mastering the knack: There is a knack in 'feeding' and once you have mastered this knack you can move backwards, and feed with considerable speed.
feeding technique, uniform strand thickness, left hand feel, adjust twist, correct overfull bite
Flat Four Plait Rope Making Technique
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Steps for flat four plait rope making:

1. Lay the four strands side by side.

2. Take the outside left-hand strand and lay it under the next strand to itself, then over what was the first strand.

3. Take the **outside right-hand...
Steps for flat four plait rope making:

1. Lay the four strands side by side.

2. Take the outside left-hand strand and lay it under the next strand to itself, then over what was the first strand.

3. Take the outside right-hand strand and lay it over the strand to its left.

4. Take the outside left strand and put it under and over the next two strands respectively, moving towards the right.

5. Thereafter, your right-hand strand goes over one strand to the left, and your left-hand strand under and over to the right, as shown in the diagrams.
flat four plait,bushcraft,rope making
Gather and Prepare Natural Fibre Materials for Rope Making
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Select suitable materials: Look for fibrous sources such as surface roots of shrubs and trees, inner bark of fallen branches or new growth (e.g., willows), water reeds and rushes, grasses, sedges, lianas, some sea weeds,...
Procedure:

1. Select suitable materials: Look for fibrous sources such as surface roots of shrubs and trees, inner bark of fallen branches or new growth (e.g., willows), water reeds and rushes, grasses, sedges, lianas, some sea weeds, fibrous leaves and stalks of palms, and fibrous-leaved plants like aloes.
2. Remove vegetable gum: If present, soak the material in water, boil it, or dry it and 'tease' it into thin strips to remove the gum.
3. Use green materials for strength: For sedges, water rushes, grasses, and lianas, use them while they are still green to ensure maximum strength.
4. Cut rather than pull: When harvesting grasses, sedges, or water rushes, cut above ground level (harvesting) instead of pulling the entire plant up (destruction).
5. Harvest selectively: Work over a wide area and leave some material for seeding and regrowth. Take only from the largest clumps.
6. Avoid destroying plants unnecessarily: Do not pull leaves off deeply rooted strong-leafed plants; they cannot be removed without damaging the plant.
7. Use palm fibre: In tropical or sub-tropical regions, harvest fibres at the junction of the leaf and trunk or from those lying on the ground beneath palms.
8. Use dead or half-dead bark for easier processing: Fibrous matter from inner bark is more easily used if the plant is dead or half-dead, as natural gum will have dried out and can be removed in a fine powder during teasing.
9. Avoid cutting live trees for bark: Only cut branches here and there if absolutely necessary; never cut an entire tree just to obtain bark for cordage.
natural fibre gathering, rope making materials, sustainable harvesting, plant fibre preparation, palm fibre collection
Laying Strands for Rope Making
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Laying Strands for Rope Making

1. Prepare the strands: The strands lie on crossbars as they are spun. When the strands have been spun to the required length, which should be no more than about 100' [30 m], they are joined together by being...
Laying Strands for Rope Making

1. Prepare the strands: The strands lie on crossbars as they are spun. When the strands have been spun to the required length, which should be no more than about 100' [30 m], they are joined together by being held at the far end.
2. Position the turner: The turner, who is facing the cranks, twists the ends together *anti-clockwise*, at the same time keeping his full weight on the rope and which is being laid up.
3. Lay the strands: The layer advances, placing the strands side by side as they turn.
4. Maintain even feeding: It is important to learn to feed the material evenly, and lay up slowly, thereby getting a smooth even rope. Do not try to rush the ropemaking. If you do, you will have uneven, badly spun strands, and ugly lays, and poor rope. Speed in ropemaking only comes with practice.
5. Initial time estimates: At first it will take a team of three or four up to two hours or more to make a 50-yard [45 m] length of rope of three lays, each of three strands, that is nine strands for a rope with a finished diameter of about 1" [2.5 cm]. With practice the same three or four people will make the same rope in 15 to 20 minutes. These times do not include time for gathering material.
6. Feeding technique: In feeding, the free ends of the strands twist in the loose material fed in by the feeder. The feeder must move backwards at a speed governed by the rate at which he feeds. As the feeder moves backwards he must keep a slight tension on the strands.
laying, strands, rope making, crossbars, anti-clockwise twist, even feeding, ropemaking speed
Make Cord by Spinning with the Fingers
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Long strong threads or fibres that have been tested for strength and pliability.

Procedure Steps:
1. Prepare Fibres: Gather the fibres into loosely held strands of even thickness. Each strand should be from 1⁄8" [3...
Materials Required:
• Long strong threads or fibres that have been tested for strength and pliability.

Procedure Steps:
1. Prepare Fibres: Gather the fibres into loosely held strands of even thickness. Each strand should be from 1⁄8" [3 mm] downwards in diameter.
2. Twist Strands Clockwise: Twist each individual strand clockwise to hold the fibre together. For a rough rule, use about 15 to 20 fibres per strand.
3. Lay Up Strands: Take two, three, or four of these strands and twist them together in an anti-clockwise direction while simultaneously twisting the remaining strands clockwise.
4. Ensure Uniformity: Make sure each strand is of equal thickness and twist. The layer (person laying up the cord) must ensure that the twisting is even, the strands are uniform, and the tension on each strand is equal.
5. Avoid Uneven Twisting: During laying, watch that no single strand twists around the others. This can happen when you're first learning to lay up.
6. Fine Cord Preparation: When making fine cords for fishing lines or snares, take extra care to keep strands uniform and the lay even. Fine cords of no more than 1⁄32" [0.75 mm] thickness can be spun with the fingers and are capable of taking a breaking strain of 20 lbs. to 30 lbs. [10 kg to 15 kg] or more.

Additional Tips:
• For longer lengths, consider using a rope-walk with multiple spinners in the form of cranks for efficiency.
• When spinning by hand, one person can twist the material by running the flat of their hand along the thigh while feeding fibre with the other hand.
• Avoid letting spun strands lie on the ground; support them on cross bars as they increase in length.
• Do not spin the material too thickly. Thick strands do not contribute to strength and may result in a weaker rope.
cord making, spinning with fingers, rope making, fibres, laying up, bushcraft, survival

Making Rope with a Single Spinner

1. Set up the material: A portion of the material is fastened to the eye of the crank, as with the multiple crank.
2. Feeding technique: The feeder holding the free ends of this strand against the...
Making Rope with a Single Spinner

1. Set up the material: A portion of the material is fastened to the eye of the crank, as with the multiple crank.
2. Feeding technique: The feeder holding the free ends of this strand against the bundle of loose material under his arm feeds in, walking backwards.
3. Supporting crossbars: Supporting crossbars, as used in a ropewalk, are required when a length of more than 20' or 30' [6 m or 9 m] is being spun.

Plaiting Techniques for Rope Making
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
One man may require a considerable length of rope, and if he has no assistance to help him spin up his material, he can often find reasonably long material (say, from 1' to 3' [30 cm to 90 cm], or more) and use this material to **plait (or...
One man may require a considerable length of rope, and if he has no assistance to help him spin up his material, he can often find reasonably long material (say, from 1' to 3' [30 cm to 90 cm], or more) and use this material to plait (or braid) and make suitable rope.

The usual three plait makes a flat rope. While quite good, it has not the finish nor shape, nor is it as 'tight' as the four or lariat plait. On other occasions, it may be necessary to plait broad bands for belts or for shoulder straps. There are many fancy braids and plaits which you can develop from these, but three plait, flat four plait, and lariat plait are basic and essential for practical woodcraft work.

A general rule for all plaits is to work from the outside into the centre.
plaiting,bushcraft,rope making
Test Material for Rope Making Suitability
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Test for strength: Pull on a length of the material to see if it can withstand force without breaking.
2. Test for pliability: Twist the material between your fingers and 'roll' the fibres together. If it stands this...
Procedure:

1. Test for strength: Pull on a length of the material to see if it can withstand force without breaking.
2. Test for pliability: Twist the material between your fingers and 'roll' the fibres together. If it stands this without snapping apart, proceed to the next test.
3. Knot test: Tie a thumb knot in the material and gently tighten it. If the material does not cut upon itself but allows the knot to be pulled taut, it is suitable for rope making.
4. Test for grip: Ensure that the fibres will 'bite' onto one another and are not smooth or slippery.

Notes:
• This procedure applies to natural materials such as plant fibres, inner barks of trees, and animal hair.
• If the material is stiff or unyielding (e.g., freshly gathered), pass it through hot flames for a few moments. The heat will cause sap to burst through cell structures, making the material pliable.
rope making, material testing, strength test, pliability test, grip test, natural fibres
Setting Up Cross Bars for a Rope Walk
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Purpose: To set up cross bars for spinning long ropes to prevent sagging, tangling, and uneven twisting.

Materials Required:
• Forked stakes
• Smooth sticks or natural materials for cross bars
• Cranks (can be made from sticks, morticed...
Purpose: To set up cross bars for spinning long ropes to prevent sagging, tangling, and uneven twisting.

Materials Required:
• Forked stakes
• Smooth sticks or natural materials for cross bars
• Cranks (can be made from sticks, morticed slabs, pegs, or bent wire)
• Connecting rods
• Fork stick for the end crank handle

Steps:
1. Drive pairs of forked stakes into the ground about 6' [1.8 m] apart and at intervals of 6' to 10' [1.8 m to 3 m].
2. Ensure cross bars are smooth and free from twigs, loose bark, or other debris that might interfere with the spinning strands.
3. Support each cross bar using two uprights and pierce it to take the cranks.
4. Use a connecting rod to enable one person to turn all cranks clockwise simultaneously.
5. At the end of the rope walk, set up a similar crank handle on a fork stick and turn it anti-clockwise to twist the connected strands together during laying up.
cross bars, rope walk, spinning ropes, bushcraft, rope making
Spinning and Laying Up Rope Strands
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Purpose: To spin and lay up rope strands to create a strong, evenly twisted rope.

Materials Required:
• Fibers (e.g., bark)
• Cranks and connecting rods
• Spinner-feeder

Steps:
1. Begin by turning the first strand clockwise, then...
Purpose: To spin and lay up rope strands to create a strong, evenly twisted rope.

Materials Required:
• Fibers (e.g., bark)
• Cranks and connecting rods
• Spinner-feeder

Steps:
1. Begin by turning the first strand clockwise, then continue laying up the strands anti-clockwise, alternating directions for each subsequent layer.
2. As you lay up the strands, keep twisting them to maintain even tension and remove any residual twist from previously spun strands.
3. If making a rope longer than can be spun in one piece:
- Lay up a section of the rope and coil it on the ground at the end of the rope walk farthest from the cranks.
- Spin new strands for the next section, then splice or marry them into the existing strands.
4. Ensure that the ends where strands are married are staggered in different places to avoid weak points in the rope.
5. Once the complete length of rope is laid up, pass it through fire to burn off loose ends and fibers for a smooth finish.
spinning rope, laying up rope, bushcraft, rope making, splicing
Testing and Finishing a Spun Rope
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Purpose: To test the strength of a spun rope and finish it for use.

Materials Required:
• Completed rope
• Fire source (e.g., fire pit or open flame)

Steps:
1. Test the rope by applying tension to ensure that individual fibers lie...
Purpose: To test the strength of a spun rope and finish it for use.

Materials Required:
• Completed rope
• Fire source (e.g., fire pit or open flame)

Steps:
1. Test the rope by applying tension to ensure that individual fibers lie lengthways along the rope, indicating proper twisting and laying up.
2. If the rope is made from bark fiber, a 1'' [2.5 cm] diameter rope can have a breaking strain of about 800 lbs. [365 kg].
3. After testing, pass the completed rope through fire to burn off loose ends and fibers. This will make the rope smooth and professional-looking.
testing rope strength, finishing rope, bushcraft, rope making

Shelter (15) -

Anchoring a Rope in Open Ground (ONE - TWO - THREE Anchor)
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
A secure anchor in open ground can be obtained by driving a stout stake well into the ground. The rope is later fastened to the base of this stake. To the head of the stake two ropes are attached, and these are tied back to the ground level of two...
A secure anchor in open ground can be obtained by driving a stout stake well into the ground. The rope is later fastened to the base of this stake. To the head of the stake two ropes are attached, and these are tied back to the ground level of two other stakes driven well into the earth a few yards behind the first stake. To the heads of these two stakes three ropes are tied and these are fanned out and tied to the bases of three other stakes driven in behind the two stakes. The main rope is now fastened to the base of the first stake.

Thatching Methods: Ground Securing and Overlapping Battens
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Secure thatching battens to the framework using ground-based methods.

Procedure:
1. Use ground securing, which involves fastening the thatching battens to the ground before laying them on the framework.
2. Once secured, lay...
Objective: Secure thatching battens to the framework using ground-based methods.

Procedure:
1. Use ground securing, which involves fastening the thatching battens to the ground before laying them on the framework.
2. Once secured, lay the battens onto the framework in an overlapping manner, similar to long tiles.
3. Ensure that each batten overlaps the previous one by a sufficient amount (typically about 1/3 of its length) to provide complete coverage and stability.
4. Secure the battens tightly at their points of overlap using natural fibers or strong plant-based cords.
5. This method is particularly useful for creating a stable base before adding thatch material.

Objective: Utilize palm stalks as thatching materials or battens.

Procedure:
1. Use palm stalks either as the thatch material itself or as thatching battens.
2. The palm stalk can be used to hold the palm leaves in position, ensuring a firm and stable structure.
3. When using palm stalks as battens, ensure they are straight and strong enough to support the weight of the thatch.
4. Secure the palm stalks tightly to the framework or other battens to prevent slippage or wind damage.
5. This method is particularly effective in areas where palm trees are abundant and provides a natural, durable solution for thatching.

Creating Loops for Netting
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
At one end of the string tie a loop and place the knot on a conveniently high nail or hook. The mesh stick is put under the loop and the needle with cord passed through as in Fig. 3. The needle and cord are passed in front of the loop formed in Fig....
At one end of the string tie a loop and place the knot on a conveniently high nail or hook. The mesh stick is put under the loop and the needle with cord passed through as in Fig. 3. The needle and cord are passed in front of the loop formed in Fig. 3 and under the original loop, while at the same time the other end of the cord is held on to the mesh stick with the thumb of the left hand. The knot is pulled taut. A succession of these loops are formed until the requisite width is reached, then this first series of loops are placed through a rod or cord, and the loops are netted on to them until the requisite length is reached.

Preparing Thatching Materials for Use
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Properly prepare thatching materials to ensure durability and effectiveness.

Steps for preparation:
1. Cut and stook long grass and reeds before use so they can season properly.
2. Allow the materials to dry out completely,...
Objective: Properly prepare thatching materials to ensure durability and effectiveness.

Steps for preparation:
1. Cut and stook long grass and reeds before use so they can season properly.
2. Allow the materials to dry out completely, as green materials tend to shrink when exposed to hot sun, leading to curling and reduced coverage value.
3. Seasoned materials will not slacken in stitches due to minimal shrinkage, ensuring a secure thatch.
4. For bracken ferns, use them while green and sew tightly; this is only recommended for emergency situations.
5. When using palm leaves, collect them early in the morning or after rain when they are softened by dew or moisture. Wetting the leaves down before sewing will soften them, allow them to lie flat, and improve coverage.
6. Avoid collecting palm leaves during hot summer days as they may be too brittle and break easily.
preparing thatching materials, seasoning grass, reeds, bracken, palm leaves, wetting down, durability

1. Ensure straightness: All wall pegs and stakes must be straight for proper use.
2. Bevel the head: The top of the stake should have a bevelled (angled) head to facilitate easier driving into the ground.
3. **Point the...
Procedure:

1. Ensure straightness: All wall pegs and stakes must be straight for proper use.
2. Bevel the head: The top of the stake should have a bevelled (angled) head to facilitate easier driving into the ground.
3. Point the foot: The bottom end of the stake should be pointed to help it penetrate the soil.
4. Avoid improper pointing: Do not point the stake with a single cut, as this can weaken the structure and reduce effectiveness.

Selecting Suitable Thatching Materials
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Choose appropriate materials for thatching based on availability and effectiveness.

Materials suitable for thatching include:
• Long grass
• Reeds
• Rushes
• Most long-stalked ferns (e.g., bracken)
• Palm leaves of all types
-...
Objective: Choose appropriate materials for thatching based on availability and effectiveness.

Materials suitable for thatching include:
• Long grass
• Reeds
• Rushes
• Most long-stalked ferns (e.g., bracken)
• Palm leaves of all types
• Pliant leafy branches (as a last resort)

Recommendations:
• Long grass and reeds are most satisfactory when used dry or partly dry. It is advisable to cut and stook them first so they can season before use.
• Bracken ferns should be thatched while green, and sewn down tightly. This method is not recommended for regular use but may be necessary in emergencies.
• Green branches are inefficient for thatching and should only be used if absolutely necessary.
• Blanches of trees or shrubs should be selected from dead branches with some leaves still on them. Shake the branch: if leaves fall immediately, it is almost useless; if they remain attached, the plant may serve effectively.
• Palm leaves are best used when dead. They can be found in abundance under palm trees and are excellent material. However, they may be brittle and break easily during hot summer days. Collect them early in the morning or after rain for better results.

Environmental benefits:
• Using bracken and reeds for thatching helps control pests (bracken is injurious to cattle; reeds choke watercourses).
thatching materials, long grass, reeds, bracken ferns, palm leaves, dead branches, environmental benefits
Construct a Camp Bed Off the Ground
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Framework similar to a table (without the table top)
• Two poles
• Sticks for cross support

Steps:
1. Build a framework: Create a framework similar to a table, using two poles and sticks for cross support.
2. **Lay...
Materials Required:
• Framework similar to a table (without the table top)
• Two poles
• Sticks for cross support

Steps:
1. Build a framework: Create a framework similar to a table, using two poles and sticks for cross support.
2. Lay the bed structure: Lay the two poles and overlay them with sticks exactly as you would for a bed on the ground.
3. Elevate the bed: Ensure that the forks (supporting points) are not as high as they would be for a table, but sufficient to elevate the bed off the ground.
4. Considerations for elevation: A camp bed should always be built off the ground in bad snake country or in areas where ground pests such as leeches, ants, scrub-mites, chiggers, or ticks are likely to be troublesome.
5. Alternative support method: Use two piles of stones to support the side poles if forked stakes and ground poles are not available.

Construct a Camp Bed Using Bags and Poles
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Two forked stakes
• Two side poles (placed into the crotches of the forked stakes at about a 45° slope)
• Two long, straight poles
• Two bags with holes cut in the bottoms to allow poles to pass through
• Dried grass or...
Materials Required:
• Two forked stakes
• Two side poles (placed into the crotches of the forked stakes at about a 45° slope)
• Two long, straight poles
• Two bags with holes cut in the bottoms to allow poles to pass through
• Dried grass or bracken fern (optional for added comfort and warmth)

Steps:
1. Set up forked stakes: Set up two forked stakes as you would for a preceding camp bed.
2. Place side poles into crotches: Place two side poles into the crotches of these forked stakes so they are on about a 45° slope.
3. Prepare bags and poles: Cut two long, straight poles and pass them through the two sides of two bags (holes cut in the bottoms of each bag to allow the poles to pass through).
4. Position the bags: The closed ends of the bags should be towards the ends of the poles, with the bags overlapping a few inches in the middle.
5. Lay the bed structure: Lay the two bed poles with the bags one on either side of the angle poles.
6. Use body weight for support: The weight of your body lying on the bags will keep the side poles pulled well down on the angle poles.
7. Add stuffing (optional): If the weather is cold or greater comfort is required, stuff dried grass or bracken fern inside the bags to provide added softness and warmth.
camp bed,bags,poles,shelter,warmth
Construct a Camp Bed Using Poles and Cross Sticks
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Two poles 6" or 7" [15 or 17.5 cm] thick, about 7' [2.1 m] long
• Pegs (about 1' [30 cm] of the peg above the pole)
• About twenty or thirty straight, strong sticks, 3½' [1.05 m] long
• Two poles 3" to 4" [7.5 to 10 cm]...
Materials Required:
• Two poles 6" or 7" [15 or 17.5 cm] thick, about 7' [2.1 m] long
• Pegs (about 1' [30 cm] of the peg above the pole)
• About twenty or thirty straight, strong sticks, 3½' [1.05 m] long
• Two poles 3" to 4" [7.5 to 10 cm] thick and 7' [2.1 m] long
• Green brushwood, fern, or waste green stuff (sucker growth, weedy bush material)

Steps:
1. Prepare the base poles: Cut two poles 6" or 7" [15 or 17.5 cm] thick and about 7' [2.1 m] long. Lay these parallel to each other, 3' [90 cm] apart.
2. Secure the base poles: Put pegs at the head and foot of the bed, driven well into the ground with about 1' [30 cm] of the peg above the pole.
3. Add cross sticks: Cut about twenty or thirty straight, strong sticks, 3½' [1.05 m] long, and lay these every 4" [10 cm] across the two poles.
4. Add top support poles: Place two poles, 3" to 4" [7.5 to 10 cm] thick and 7' [2.1 m] long, on top of the cross sticks. These should lie against the pegs driven in to hold the two "bed" poles secure.
5. Add additional cross sticks at the head: At the head end of the bed, lay about half a dozen cross sticks on top of these last two poles.
6. Add bedding material: Cut green brushwood, fern, or waste green stuff and place it so that the main stalks are lengthways along the bed. Pile it high between the two top poles and lying across the cross sticks.
7. Optional long-term use: If you are going to be in camp for a long period, make yourself a camp mattress from grass on the camp loom. If bedding is short, weave a covering from dried grass on the same loom.
camp bed,poles,cross sticks,bedding material,shelter
Construct a Stick Hammock Using a Camp Loom
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Camp loom
• Vines, twisted bark fibres, grass rope, or any suitable material for weaving
• Sticks about 1" [5 cm] thick for cross parts
• Grass mattress (optional for covering)

Steps:
1. Set up the camp loom: Use a...
Materials Required:
• Camp loom
• Vines, twisted bark fibres, grass rope, or any suitable material for weaving
• Sticks about 1" [5 cm] thick for cross parts
• Grass mattress (optional for covering)

Steps:
1. Set up the camp loom: Use a camp loom to set up the hammock.
2. Weave the hammock: Weave the hammock using vines, twisted bark fibres, grass rope, or any suitable material for weaving. Use sticks about 1" [5 cm] thick for cross parts.
3. Size specifications: The hammock should be at least 3' [90 cm] wide by 6½' [195 cm] long.
4. Add spreaders and suspension ropes: The end two spreaders should be 2' [5 cm] thick, with short lengths of rope brought to the central rope for suspension. Ropes from each of the four corners will also serve to suspend the hammock.
5. Optional cover: A grass mattress woven on the camp loom makes an excellent cover for the hammock.
stick hammock,camp loom,weaving,shelter,grass mattress
Folding a Flag for 'Breaking'
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
The flag is folded neatly along its length four to eight times, and then the fly is either folded in concertina fashion or rolled towards the hoist. The toggle is uppermost on the hoist, and the halyard is on the lower side of the hoist. The halyard...
The flag is folded neatly along its length four to eight times, and then the fly is either folded in concertina fashion or rolled towards the hoist. The toggle is uppermost on the hoist, and the halyard is on the lower side of the hoist. The halyard is wrapped tightly round the flag, and then bent, and the loop pressed under the last rollings, held by the pressure of the cord against the bunting of the flag. The toggle is fastened to the eye of the halyard on the mast, and the free end of the flag hoist is fastened to the other end of the mast halyard with a double sheet or crossover bend.
folding flag,breaking,hoist,halyard,toggle
Create Useful Items from Dead Timber in Camp
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Use dead wood: Preferably use dead (but not rotten) timber for making items like pegs, stakes, forks, or hooks.
2. Cut from windblown branches: Look for suitable materials close to your camp site, such as windblown...
Procedure:

1. Use dead wood: Preferably use dead (but not rotten) timber for making items like pegs, stakes, forks, or hooks.
2. Cut from windblown branches: Look for suitable materials close to your camp site, such as windblown branches that are already fallen.
3. Avoid cutting green wood: Avoid cutting live, growing wood to help conserve the forest and reduce unnecessary damage.
4. Use a sharp tool: A good machete is essential for efficiently cutting and shaping dead timber into useful items.
5. Apply common sense: Use your judgment when selecting materials and constructing items in camp.
dead wood,campcraft,tools,construction,bushcraft
Set Up a Comfortable Camp Using Natural Resources
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Use available materials: With only a machete or sharp knife, it is possible to set up a comfortable camp using natural resources found around the area.
2. Create necessary items: Construct bed frames, tables, seats,...
Procedure:

1. Use available materials: With only a machete or sharp knife, it is possible to set up a comfortable camp using natural resources found around the area.
2. Create necessary items: Construct bed frames, tables, seats, chairs, cooking areas, and lighting from local materials such as branches, stones, and other natural elements.
3. Improve comfort: A properly made camp bed can be as restful as an inner spring mattress, and food cooked outdoors is often more flavorful.
4. Avoid discomfort: Proper preparation helps prevent issues like wet wood that won't take fire, wind blowing heat away from cooking pots, or pests finding your food supply.
camping,natural resources,camp setup,bushcraft

Join Green Materials Safely

2. Alternative methods: If knots are not suitable,...
Procedure:

1. Choose the right knot: An overhand knot is often sufficient for joining green materials, but if the material tends to cut itself, use a sheet bend or reef knot instead.
2. Alternative methods: If knots are not suitable, consider plaiting or spinning the materials into rope as an alternative method of joining them.
green materials,knots,joining,sheet bend,reef knot
Properly Prepare Sedges and Bulrushes for Use
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Handle with care: When working with sword grasses or sedges, wear a pair of socks on your hands to protect against the razor-sharp edges that can cause cuts which may become infected.
2. Use appropriate knots: Green...
Procedure:

1. Handle with care: When working with sword grasses or sedges, wear a pair of socks on your hands to protect against the razor-sharp edges that can cause cuts which may become infected.
2. Use appropriate knots: Green materials like sedges and bulrushes require special knots for lashing due to their natural springiness. The timber hitch is recommended as the starting point for all lashings, not a clove hitch.
3. Avoid improper knot placement: Ensure that the free end of the material passes straight through the 'eye' when tying a lashing and does not come back against it, as this can cause the material to cut itself.
4. Tighten properly: Make three to six complete turns around the two poles while tightening each turn to ensure the lashing is secure.
5. Use frapping turns: After making the initial turns, apply frapping turns to close and tighten the entire lashing.
6. Finish securely: Pass the free end of the material through an opening in the lashing and finish with a couple of half hitches pulled tight.
sedges,bulrushes,knots,green materials,lashing,timber hitch

After pulling taut, the two free ends are cut close in and the whole binding is smooth and neat.
whipping,cord,knot,binding
Diagonal Lashing Procedure
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Diagonal Lashing is used for bracing or joining spars at irregular angles.

### Steps:
1. Start with a timber hitch or a clove hitch and take about three or four full turns vertically.
2. Pass the rope under the top spar and make...
Diagonal Lashing is used for bracing or joining spars at irregular angles.

### Steps:
1. Start with a timber hitch or a clove hitch and take about three or four full turns vertically.
2. Pass the rope under the top spar and make about three to four full turns horizontally.
3. Make two or three frapping turns to pull the lashing tight.
4. Secure by either:
- Using two half-hitches on the pole, or
- Passing the end between the lashing and the pole, then securing with a half hitch on the lashing.
diagonal lashing, timber hitch, clove hitch, frapping turns, half-hitches

How to Finish Off a Broad Plait
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Steps to Finish Off a Broad Plait:

1. Fold back the first strand: Take one of the centre strands which it enclosed in and lay it back upon itself as shown in Fig. 1.
2. Weave the folded strand: Weave this folded strand back upon itself...
Steps to Finish Off a Broad Plait:

1. Fold back the first strand: Take one of the centre strands which it enclosed in and lay it back upon itself as shown in Fig. 1.
2. Weave the folded strand: Weave this folded strand back upon itself as shown in Fig. 2.
3. Use opposite side strand: Take a strand from the opposite side, lay it back, and weave it between the strands already plaited.
4. Ensure even pattern: All the strands should be woven back so that no strands show an uneven pattern, with a regular under-over-under of the alternating weaves.
5. Use a chisel edge tool: If you have plaited tightly and encounter difficulty in working the loose ends between the plaited strands, sharpen a thin piece of wood to a chisel edge and use it to open the strands sufficiently.
6. Smooth the plait: After finishing off, roll under a bottle to work smooth.
finishing plait,knotting,bushcraft,rope tying
How to Tie a Round or Lariat Plait (Four Strands)
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Steps to Tie a Round or Lariat Plait with Four Strands:

1. Start with four strands: Lay the four strands together side by side as shown in Fig. 1.
2. First crossing: Cross the right-hand centre strand over and then around the left-hand...
Steps to Tie a Round or Lariat Plait with Four Strands:

1. Start with four strands: Lay the four strands together side by side as shown in Fig. 1.
2. First crossing: Cross the right-hand centre strand over and then around the left-hand strand (refer to Fig. 1).
3. Pass left-hand outside strand: Take the left-hand outside strand and pass it over the two crossed strands, then under the right-hand one of the two so that it points towards the left as shown in Fig. 2.
4. Repeat with free right-hand strand: Take the free right-hand strand and pass it over the two twisted strands to the left and completely round the left-hand one of the two as shown in Fig. 3.
5. Repeat with right-hand strand again: Repeat this process with the right-hand strand as shown in Fig. 4.
6. Final plaiting: Continue repeating until the finished plait looks like the pattern in Fig. 5.
round plait,lariat plait,knotting,bushcraft,rope tying
Long Splicing Procedure
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Long Splicing is used to join the ends of a rope without significantly thickening it, allowing it to pass through a sheave.

### Steps:
1. Unlay the strands for a considerable length and then marry them as in a short splice.
2. Unlay one...
Long Splicing is used to join the ends of a rope without significantly thickening it, allowing it to pass through a sheave.

### Steps:
1. Unlay the strands for a considerable length and then marry them as in a short splice.
2. Unlay one strand and lay its married counterpart along its place in the rope.
3. The two centers are held with a crossover knot, and the strands are thinned down and spliced as for a short splice.
4. Finish the end strands with a crossover knot again, thinning them down and finishing as for a short splice.
long splicing, unlaying strands, crossover knot, rope joining

Short Splicing Procedure
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Short Splicing is used to join the ends of a rope together without significantly increasing its diameter.

### Steps:
1. Unlay the strands and bring them together so that they are butted hard up against each other.
2. The strand D first goes...
Short Splicing is used to join the ends of a rope together without significantly increasing its diameter.

### Steps:
1. Unlay the strands and bring them together so that they are butted hard up against each other.
2. The strand D first goes under the standing end of A, then over strand B, and over C on the standing end.
3. Each strand at either end should go over one strand of the standing end on the opposite side and under the next strand so that there is a strand of the standing end between each short side of the splice.
4. Continue working the free strand of each end four or five times into the strands of the standing end.
short splicing, unlaying strands, rope joining
Square Lashing Procedure
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Square Lashing is used to join poles at right angles.

### Steps:
1. Start with a timber hitch or a clove hitch below the cross bar. If using a timber hitch, ensure the pull is straight through the eye and not back from it. Pulling back...
Square Lashing is used to join poles at right angles.

### Steps:
1. Start with a timber hitch or a clove hitch below the cross bar. If using a timber hitch, ensure the pull is straight through the eye and not back from it. Pulling back will cut the lashing material.
2. Wrap the lashing material tightly around both the upright and cross bar about four complete times.
3. Make two or three frapping turns, which are turns that go round the lashing and pull it taut. These help secure the lashing.
4. Secure the end of the frapping turns either by half-hitches or by passing the end between the lashing at the crossover and securing with a half hitch.
square lashing, timber hitch, clove hitch, frapping turns, half-hitches
Whipping a Cord
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Before the finish of the binding, a loop formed from the end is laid under the binding at the start. This end is bent back to form a loop and the last six to twelve turns bind over this loop. At the last turn of the binding, the cord is put through...
Before the finish of the binding, a loop formed from the end is laid under the binding at the start. This end is bent back to form a loop and the last six to twelve turns bind over this loop. At the last turn of the binding, the cord is put through the loop and the free end of the loop is pulled tightly, thus drawing the end of the binding beneath the last turn.
whipping,cord,knot,binding

Purpose: To boil water quickly in an open space with high winds by using ground placement of the billy.

Materials Needed:
• Cooking vessel (billy)
• Firewood

Steps:
1. Place Billy on Ground: Place your billy directly on the ground where you want to boil water.
2. Build Fire Around It: Construct a fire on both sides of the billy and to its windward side so that the wind is drawn over it, blowing hot flames around the sides of the billy.
3. Boil Water: The wind will help accelerate the boiling process by directing heat efficiently toward the billy.

Notes:
• This method is useful in high-wind conditions where suspending the billy may be unsafe or inefficient.
• Ensure that the fire is built securely and monitored closely to prevent accidents.
ground billy boiling, windproof cooking, survival cooking

Purpose: To build a reflector fireplace that improves fire efficiency in windy conditions.

Materials Needed:
• Stones or flat rocks
• Firewood

Steps:
1. Choose Location: Select a location where the wind is blowing consistently, and construct your fire on the leeward side of the reflector.
2. Build Reflector Wall: Construct a wall using stones or flat rocks on the windward side of the fire to act as a reflector.
3. Light Fire: Light your fire in front of the reflector wall so that the wind is drawn over it, directing flames upward and across the fire for improved efficiency.

Notes:
• This method helps improve fire efficiency by using the wind to draw heat and flames upward.
• Ensure that the reflector wall is stable and secure before lighting your fire.

Materials Needed:
• Stones (not from watercourses)
• A flat area for construction

Steps:

2. Build the Wall: Construct a wall around the fire using stones. The wall should be about 9" or 10" [23 or 25 cm] high.
3. Orientation of Opening: Ensure that the opening is parallel to the valley for optimal wind flow and heat distribution.

Notes:
• This method helps contain the fire and improve efficiency by directing airflow.
• Avoid using stones from watercourses due to potential safety risks.

Making Billy Hooks for Safe Cooking Vessel Suspension

• Sticks or branches (about ½" in diameter)
• A sharp tool for cutting and notching

1. Select Sticks: Choose...
Purpose: To create safe and effective hooks for suspending cooking vessels (billies) over a fire.

Materials Needed:
• Sticks or branches (about ½" in diameter)
• A sharp tool for cutting and notching

Steps:
1. Select Sticks: Choose straight sticks or branches that are about ½" in diameter, with lengths varying from 6" to 10" [15 to 25 cm].
2. Cut Hook Shape: Cut a hooked shape at one end of each stick.
3. Make Nick on Opposite Side: On the side opposite to the hook, cut a single deep nick into the wood so that the direction of the cut is away from the hook.
4. Use for Suspension: Insert the wire handle of your billy into this nick and hang it from the stick above the fire.

Notes:
• The nick ensures that the billy is securely held in place without being burned through by heat.
• This method improves safety when suspending cooking vessels over a fire.
