3. Adjust Height: By changing the base of the tripods, you can adjust the height of the billy above the flames as needed.

Notes:
• This method allows for flexibility in adjusting the height of your cooking vessel above the fire.
• Ensure that the tripod structure is stable and secure before use.
tripod billy support, fire cooking, survival cooking

Archery (10) -

Proper Arrow Grip and Shooting Form
Standard Source: Boy Scout Handbook 1911
Grip:
• Use the archer's grip, not the ordinary finger and thumb pinch
• The thumb and little finger are not used in the grip

Shooting Position:
1. Stand perfectly straight with feet planted so that the **centres of the heels are in...
Grip:
• Use the archer's grip, not the ordinary finger and thumb pinch
• The thumb and little finger are not used in the grip

Shooting Position:
1. Stand perfectly straight with feet planted so that the centres of the heels are in line with the target
2. Grasp the bow in the middle with the left hand
3. Place the arrow on the string at the left side of the bow
4. Hold the bow plumb (vertical)
5. Draw the bow until the notch of the arrow is right under your eye, and the head of the arrow points back to the bow
6. Ensure the right elbow is in line with the arrow
7. Release the arrow by straightening the fingers slightly, turning the hand outward at the bottom and drawing it back one inch
8. Always release the arrow in exactly the same way for consistent accuracy
9. Keep the left hand still until the arrow strikes the target

Distance:
• Begin shooting at 15 to 20 yards for practice
• For a four-foot target, shoot from 40 yards when well-practiced
• For a three-foot target, shoot from 30 yards
arrow grip,shooting form,archery technique,proper stance,release technique
Choosing Arrow Materials for Different Uses
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Procedure:

1. Wooden arrows are less expensive and suitable for both hunting and target shooting.
2. They are recommended for novice archers due to their lower cost and durability.
3. Fiberglass arrows are preferred for hunting because...
Procedure:

1. Wooden arrows are less expensive and suitable for both hunting and target shooting.
2. They are recommended for novice archers due to their lower cost and durability.
3. Fiberglass arrows are preferred for hunting because of their ruggedness and durability.
4. Aluminum arrows are preferred for target shooting due to their lighter weight and precision.

Details:
• Each material has specific advantages depending on the use case (hunting vs. target).
• Match arrow materials with intended purpose for optimal performance.
arrow material, wood, fiberglass, aluminum, hunting, target shooting

Materials:
• For hunting: hickory or ash
• For target use: solid heartwood of hard pine

Dimensions:
• Length: 25 inches (hunting) or 28 inches (target)
• Thickness: 3/8 inch (hunting) or 5/16 inch (target)

Feathers:
• Use turkey and goose wing feathers
• Cut three pieces, each 2 to 3 inches long from the midrib of the feather
• Attach feathers with glue or lash them using the midrib for durability
• The cockfeather should be positioned away from the bow when shooting and marked for easy identification

Arrowhead:
• Use a ferrule head (like an umbrella top) to prevent splitting
• For small game, use a knob of hard wood or the knuckle bone of a small animal

Finishing Touches:
• Paint arrows for preservation, identification, and visibility when lost
• Use bright colors for better visibility

Selecting Proper Arrow Length for Archery
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Procedure:

1. Measure the arrow length from the base of the pyle (point) to the bottom of the nock.
2. For broadhead hunting arrows, measure from a point three-quarters of an inch in front of the base of the broadhead to the bottom of the...
Procedure:

1. Measure the arrow length from the base of the pyle (point) to the bottom of the nock.
2. For broadhead hunting arrows, measure from a point three-quarters of an inch in front of the base of the broadhead to the bottom of the nock.
3. If choosing between arrows that are slightly too short or too long, select those that are slightly longer.

Details:
• Arrow length must be tailored to the archer's physical dimensions.
• Proper arrow length ensures consistent accuracy and performance.
arrow length, archery, bow, spine, nock, pyle
Understanding Arrow Spine and Flexibility
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Procedure:

1. The spine of an arrow refers to its flexibility or stiffness.
2. The spine must be matched to the archer’s bow weight for proper performance.
3. If the arrow is too stiff (high spine) or too flexible (low spine), it will not...
Procedure:

1. The spine of an arrow refers to its flexibility or stiffness.
2. The spine must be matched to the archer’s bow weight for proper performance.
3. If the arrow is too stiff (high spine) or too flexible (low spine), it will not fly straight, causing the shot to go off-target (left or right).

Details:
• Arrows are manufactured with specific spine ratings for different bow weights.
• Better quality arrows are matched for weight and spine to ensure consistency.
arrow spine, archery, bow, flexibility, stiffness
Understanding Arrowhead Types and Uses
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Procedure:

1. Broadheads are used for hunting large game.
- They are about three inches long and one-and-a-quarter inches wide.
- Made of tempered steel with two, three, or four cutting edges that must be honed to razor sharpness.
2....
Procedure:

1. Broadheads are used for hunting large game.
- They are about three inches long and one-and-a-quarter inches wide.
- Made of tempered steel with two, three, or four cutting edges that must be honed to razor sharpness.
2. Blunt arrowheads are used for small game.
- Have a flat face and kill by the shock of impact rather than penetration.
3. Other common points include target, field, and fish points, each designed for specific purposes.
4. A special type is the flu-flu arrow, designed for wing shooting.
- Fletched with a fluffy cluster of feathers to ensure short-range accuracy.
- Designed to transmit shock as well as cut and penetrate.

Details:
• Broadheads must be kept sharp for effective hunting.
• Flu-flu arrows are only suitable for highly skilled archers due to the difficulty of wing shooting.
arrowhead types, broadheads, blunt points, flu-flu arrow, hunting, target shooting

Preparing Octopus for Consumption
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:
1. Identify edible octopus species (avoid small ringed octopuses 1" to 6" [2.5 to 15 cm] long, which can deliver fatal stings).
2. Remove the tentacles from the octopus.
3. Beat the tentacles thoroughly with a blunt object to...
Procedure:
1. Identify edible octopus species (avoid small ringed octopuses 1" to 6" [2.5 to 15 cm] long, which can deliver fatal stings).
2. Remove the tentacles from the octopus.
3. Beat the tentacles thoroughly with a blunt object to tenderize them.
4. Boil the tentacles in very hot oil for 10-15 minutes until fully cooked.

Notes:
• The flesh of octopus tastes like lobster.
• This method is commonly used by Mediterranean peoples.
• Always exercise caution when handling any octopus species.
octopus, cooking, foraging, survival food, edible sea creatures

Fire Energy (8) -

Create and Use a Firebow Set for Friction Fire Lighting
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Bow
• Thong (made from leather lace or greenhide belt lacing)
• Drill (same wood as footpiece, recommended: willows or non-resinous pines)
• Footpiece (with shallow hole and undercut V)
• Headpiece (with a shallow hole,...
Materials Required:
• Bow
• Thong (made from leather lace or greenhide belt lacing)
• Drill (same wood as footpiece, recommended: willows or non-resinous pines)
• Footpiece (with shallow hole and undercut V)
• Headpiece (with a shallow hole, lubricated with graphite, fat, or ear wax)
• Tinder
• Waterproof bag for storing tinder

Steps to Prepare the Firebow Set:
1. Prepare the Footpiece:
- Cut a shallow hole into the upper side about ½" [12 mm] from one edge.
- Make an undercut V in the edge of this hole, at least 1/8" [3 mm] deep.

2. Prepare the Headpiece:
- Bore a shallow hole into the underside.
- Lubricate the hole with graphite (from a pencil), fat, or ear wax.

3. Assemble the Firebow Set:
- Place the drill through both the footpiece and headpiece holes.
- Ensure the thong is wrapped around the drill correctly: *the drill must be on the outer side of the thong, with the portion of the thong nearest the bow handle on the upper side of the drill*.

4. Adjust Tension:
- The thong should be tied to the bow with a thumb knot at the tip and wound around the handle in half hitches for tension control.

Proper Body Positioning:
• Kneel on your right knee, with the ball of your left foot on the footpiece to hold it firmly to the ground.
• Place the lower end of the drill in the hole of the footpiece and the top end in the hole of the headpiece.
• Hold the headpiece with your left hand, bracing your wrist against the shin of your left leg for stability.
• Hold the bow in your right hand, with your little and third fingers outside the thong to adjust tension.

Fire Lighting Procedure:
1. Test the Wood:
- Rotate the drill slowly using the bow to generate friction.
- Observe if a fine brown powder (called 'punk') is ground out from the footpiece hole.
- Test the punk: it should feel slightly gritty and become silky smooth when rubbed between fingers. This indicates suitable wood for firemaking.

2. Prepare Tinder:
- Place a generous bundle of tinder under the undercut V in the footpiece.

3. Generate Heat:
- Continue rotating the drill with the bow, applying light pressure on the headpiece until smoke begins to rise from the footpiece hole.
- Increase pressure and give 20–30 faster strokes with the bow.

4. Extract the Coal:
- Lift the drill cleanly from the footpiece.
- Fold some of the tinder over lightly and blow gently into the V cut.
- If a blue thread of smoke continues to rise, you have created a glowing coal (likely visible as red).

5. Ignite the Tinder:
- Fold the tinder completely over the footpiece and continue blowing into the mass until it bursts into flame.

Tips for Success:
• Some authorities recommend placing a little charcoal or gritty material in the footpiece hole to help generate more punk and spark quickly.
• Always carry tinder in a waterproof bag to protect it from moisture.
• Use the same type of wood for both the drill and footpiece (e.g., willows, non-resinous pines) for optimal results.
fire by friction,bow and drill,footpiece,headpiece,tinder,punk,firebow set
Alternative Methods to Strike Fire
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
If flint and steel are unavailable, consider these alternatives:

• Iron Pyrites:
- Two pieces of iron pyrites can be struck together to produce hot sparks that last for at least a second.
- Iron pyrites is commonly found in nature and easy...
If flint and steel are unavailable, consider these alternatives:

• Iron Pyrites:
- Two pieces of iron pyrites can be struck together to produce hot sparks that last for at least a second.
- Iron pyrites is commonly found in nature and easy to obtain.
• Quartz and Steel:
- Quartz and steel, or two pieces of quartz, can also strike off good sparks, though they are harder to use than flint and steel.

Note: Sparks must fall directly onto prepared tinders, which should then be blown into a coal and eventually a flame.
iron,pyrites,quartz,strike,spark,alternative
Prepare Natural Tinders for Fire Starting
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
To prepare natural tinders for fire starting:

1. Use dry materials such as beaten grass, finely teased bark, or palm fibre.
2. Beat and pound the material until the fibres are fine and soft to improve its ability to take and hold a spark.
3....
To prepare natural tinders for fire starting:

1. Use dry materials such as beaten grass, finely teased bark, or palm fibre.
2. Beat and pound the material until the fibres are fine and soft to improve its ability to take and hold a spark.
3. Optionally, add a light dusting of very finely ground charcoal for improved fire-catching properties.
4. For even better results, thoroughly scorch the tinders.
5. If saltpetre is available, mix it with the charcoal before adding it to the tinder or soak the tinder in a solution of saltpetre and water, then allow it to dry completely.

Important: Tinder impregnated with saltpetre must be stored in an airtight container to prevent dampness from the air.
prepare,tinder,fire,starting,charcoal,saltpetre,scorch
Striking Fire from Flint and Steel
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
To strike fire using flint and steel:

1. Use synthetic flint (found in cigarette lighters) for better results than natural flint.
2. Press two pieces of synthetic flint into a small piece of 'perspex' to create an emergency firelighting tool:
-...
To strike fire using flint and steel:

1. Use synthetic flint (found in cigarette lighters) for better results than natural flint.
2. Press two pieces of synthetic flint into a small piece of 'perspex' to create an emergency firelighting tool:
- Heat the perspex and press the flints in while it is hot.
- Hold under cold water so the perspex shrinks around the flints and holds them securely.
3. Strike the flint against steel to produce sparks that fall onto prepared tinders.
4. Blow gently on the tinders to create a coal, then a flame.

Note: Only a pinch of tinder is needed if you are proficient with this method.
flint,steel,strike,spark,tinder
Use Old Cotton or Linen Rag as Tinder
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Old cotton or linen rags can be used as highly effective tinders:

1. Scorch the rag black.
2. Tease it into fine fibres.
3. Use a pinch of this material when striking a spark, as it will reliably catch and quickly become a glowing...
Old cotton or linen rags can be used as highly effective tinders:

1. Scorch the rag black.
2. Tease it into fine fibres.
3. Use a pinch of this material when striking a spark, as it will reliably catch and quickly become a glowing coal.

Note: This is one of the most reliable natural tinders available in survival situations.
cotton,rag,tinder,scorch,effective
Create a 'Slow Match' from Soft Inner Bark
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
To create a slow-burning match (slow match) for fire starting:

1. Identify plants with soft inner bark that smoulder slowly when lighted.
2. Tease and spin the bark into cord.
3. Use lengths of this cord as a 'slow match' to maintain a coal over an...
To create a slow-burning match (slow match) for fire starting:

1. Identify plants with soft inner bark that smoulder slowly when lighted.
2. Tease and spin the bark into cord.
3. Use lengths of this cord as a 'slow match' to maintain a coal over an extended period, saving matches in survival situations.

Note: This is particularly useful for maintaining fire without frequent relighting.
slow,match,bark,smoulder,cord
Light Two Fires with One Match Using Split Matches
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
How to split a wooden match:
1. Push the point of a pin or sharp knife immediately below the head of the match.
2. Force down sharply - the head will split in two, and the wood will run off or split.
3. You now have two heads with enough wood...
How to split a wooden match:
1. Push the point of a pin or sharp knife immediately below the head of the match.
2. Force down sharply - the head will split in two, and the wood will run off or split.
3. You now have two heads with enough wood left on one half to burn for a second or more, long enough to start tinder blazing.

How to split a paper match:
1. Start to split the match at the end away from the head by peeling the paper towards the head.
2. This will split the head, and you now have two matches, each with a head on one side only.

How to split a wax match:
1. Split the match from the end away from the head and up to the head.
2. It may be necessary to use a knife to split the actual head itself.

How to strike a split match:
1. Hold the 'stalk' of the match between the thumb and middle finger, with the tip of the forefinger resting lightly on the head of the match.
2. Draw the match flatly along the striking surface.
3. Immediately the head starts to burn, lift the forefinger that has been holding it gently down to the striking surface.
4. Allow the match to flame.

Tips:
• It requires practice to be certain that you can always split your match and strike both portions.
• During wartime, prisoners were able to split a match into six portions and strike each one with certainty.
split matches,light two fires,one match,bushcraft fire lighting

Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
To test if natural materials can serve as effective tinders:

1. Take a loosely teased handful of the material.
2. Place a coal from the fireplace into the material.
3. Blow gently on the material.
4. If the fire from the coal extends to the...
To test if natural materials can serve as effective tinders:

1. Take a loosely teased handful of the material.
2. Place a coal from the fireplace into the material.
3. Blow gently on the material.
4. If the fire from the coal extends to the tinders, they are suitable for use.

Note: This test helps identify which natural materials can be used as tinders in survival situations.
tinder,test,combustibility,natural,tinder,test

Fish Cleaning (7) -

Cut Fish Into Steaks
Reference Source: How To Clean A Fish
### How to Cut a Fish into Steaks

1. Using a sharp, heavy knife or cleaver, cut through the fish to make steaks of the desired thickness, usually an inch or so.
2. Try to make the cuts even and clean.
3. If the central bone gives you...
### How to Cut a Fish into Steaks

1. Using a sharp, heavy knife or cleaver, cut through the fish to make steaks of the desired thickness, usually an inch or so.
2. Try to make the cuts even and clean.
3. If the central bone gives you trouble, rest the knife against it and whack the back of the knife with a wooden or rubber mallet.
steak cutting,fish cleaning,bushcraft
Make a Long Cut Along the Back of the Fish
Reference Source: How To Clean A Fish
### How to Make a Long Cut Along the Back of the Fish

1. Lay the fish on its side, and make a top-to-bottom cut behind the gill cover.
2. With the back of the fish toward you, make a long cut along the back from the gill cover to the...
### How to Make a Long Cut Along the Back of the Fish

1. Lay the fish on its side, and make a top-to-bottom cut behind the gill cover.
2. With the back of the fish toward you, make a long cut along the back from the gill cover to the tail, following the line of the central bone.
3. Lift the flesh as you cut to reveal the central bone and make the job easier.
back cutting,fish cleaning,bushcraft
Remove Fish Gills
Reference Source: How To Clean A Fish
### How to Remove Fish Gills

1. Snip the gills at the bottom with a knife or scissors to detach them.
2. Next, snip the gills at the top and remove them with your fingers. They can be sharp, so be careful.
gill removal,fish cleaning,bushcraft
Remove Pin Bones from Fish
Reference Source: How To Clean A Fish
### How to Remove Pin Bones from Fish

1. Use needle-nose or similar pliers, as shown, to remove pin bones.
2. Alternatively, cut a 'zip-strip' by making a V-shaped cut on either side of the bones and pulling out the V with your fingers.
pin bone removal,fish cleaning,bushcraft
Remove Skin from Fillet
Reference Source: How To Clean A Fish
### How to Remove Skin from a Fillet

1. Make a small cut at the narrow end of the fillet and grab the skin.
2. Use a sharp knife, held parallel to the skin, to run between the skin and flesh.
fillet skinning,fish cleaning,bushcraft
Remove Thin Belly Flap Bones from Fish
Reference Source: How To Clean A Fish
### How to Remove Thin Belly Flap Bones from Fish

1. The thin belly flap may contain a row of bones.
2. Use your fillet knife to cut underneath them from each side, forming a V shape.
3. Lift the V out entirely.
belly flap removal,fish cleaning,bushcraft
Skin a Fish
Reference Source: How To Clean A Fish
### How to Skin a Fish

1. Turn the fish so that the belly is toward you, and make a long cut from gill to tail.
2. The tip of the knife should meet the first cut at the central bone, and the fillet will be released.
fish skinning,fish cleaning,bushcraft

Rope Handling (7) -

Climbing a Cliff Face Using the Footlock
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
When climbing up a bush-made rope always use the footlock, and when descending never slide down the rope. Climb down again using the same footlock.

The footlock offers a measure of safety, and the climber is so secure that he can actually stand...
When climbing up a bush-made rope always use the footlock, and when descending never slide down the rope. Climb down again using the same footlock.

The footlock offers a measure of safety, and the climber is so secure that he can actually stand on the rope and rest without his body weight being carried entirely on his arms. To prove this, use the footlock, and clasp the rope to your body with your arms. You will find that you are 'standing' on the rope and quite secure.

By means of the footlock you can climb to any height on the rope, stopping to rest when your arms tire.
footlock,bushcraft,climbing techniques
Descending a Cliff Face Using the Footlock
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
In descending, the body is bent, the hands lowered, and the footlock released, and a fresh grip taken with the feet at a lower level on the rope.

This method of descending is much safer than sliding. In sliding there is grave risk of bad rope burns...
In descending, the body is bent, the hands lowered, and the footlock released, and a fresh grip taken with the feet at a lower level on the rope.

This method of descending is much safer than sliding. In sliding there is grave risk of bad rope burns to hands and legs.
descending,bushcraft,footlock
Footwear Recommendations for Rope Climbing and Descending
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
It is advisable to wear boots or shoes when climbing bushmade ropes.

When using the 'Absyle' it will be found that bare feet, sandshoes or spiked shoes give a better grip on the rock face than plain leather soles.
footwear,bushcraft,safety
How to Make the Footlock
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
The footlock is made by holding onto the rope with both hands, lifting the knees, and kicking the rope to the outside of one foot. The foot on the opposite side to the rope is 'pointed' so that the toe picks up the rope, which is pulled over the...
The footlock is made by holding onto the rope with both hands, lifting the knees, and kicking the rope to the outside of one foot. The foot on the opposite side to the rope is 'pointed' so that the toe picks up the rope, which is pulled over the foot which was against the rope, and under the instep of the foot which 'picked' it up.

The two feet are brought together, and the rope is now over the instep of one foot, and under the ball of the other. Then, to secure the grip, and lock the rope the feet are placed one on top of the other so that the rope is clamped down by the foot on top.

By straightening the knees, and raising the hands, the body is lifted, and a fresh grip taken for the next rise.
footlock,bushcraft,knot tying
Testing Your Rope for Safety
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Prior to trusting your life to a bush-made rope, always test it. Tie one end to a tree and put three or four fellows onto the other end. Have them take the strain gently until finally all their weight is on the rope. If they cannot break it, then it...
Prior to trusting your life to a bush-made rope, always test it. Tie one end to a tree and put three or four fellows onto the other end. Have them take the strain gently until finally all their weight is on the rope. If they cannot break it, then it is safe for one man at a time to use it to climb or descend a cliff face.
testing rope,bushcraft,rope safety
Using the 'Absyle' for Ascent on a Sloped Rock Face
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
When ascending a rock face which has an extreme slope but is not vertical, the feet are 'walked' up the rock face, the body is pulled up the rope and the slack, hanging below the legs, is pulled up with one hand and fed over the shoulder. By this...
When ascending a rock face which has an extreme slope but is not vertical, the feet are 'walked' up the rock face, the body is pulled up the rope and the slack, hanging below the legs, is pulled up with one hand and fed over the shoulder. By this means the climber can 'sit' on the rope and rest.
absyle,bushcraft,ascent techniques
Using the 'Absyle' for Rock Descent
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
The 'Absyle' is used for rock work, generally for descending, though it can be used on some faces for ascent. In the 'Absyle' the body is up-right, but the legs are stretched out, and the feet pressed against the rock face.

The rope passes down...
The 'Absyle' is used for rock work, generally for descending, though it can be used on some faces for ascent. In the 'Absyle' the body is up-right, but the legs are stretched out, and the feet pressed against the rock face.

The rope passes down between the thighs, around one thigh and diagonally up and across the upper half of the body and over the shoulder opposite to the leg under which it passes. The rope may be gripped with one hand.

In descending, the free hand pulls the rope over the shoulder. This leaves a loop below the thigh, and the feet are 'walking' down the rock face until the thigh is again snug in the loop. The 'Absyle' used for descending makes it practically impossible to fall.
absyle,bushcraft,rock descent

Construct a Bush Windlass for Heavy Rope Strain
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Select a site where a tree forks low to the ground, with the fork facing the direction of required pull.
2. Alternatively, drive in a stout fork and anchor it using the '1-2-3' method.
3. Use a forked log as the windlass portion;...
Procedure:

1. Select a site where a tree forks low to the ground, with the fork facing the direction of required pull.
2. Alternatively, drive in a stout fork and anchor it using the '1-2-3' method.
3. Use a forked log as the windlass portion; notch the forks to take a lever (up to 7' [2.1 m] long).
4. Pass the rope around the roller several times so that it locks upon itself.
5. If the fork of the roller is long, the rope may pass through the fork.
6. This type of bush windlass can be used for various heavy-duty tasks.
windlass, rope, anchor, forked log, lever, bushcraft

Construct a Bush Hoe from a Forked Branch

Materials Needed:
• A dead or half-dead branch of hardwood
• A side branch (5' to 6' [1.5 to 1.8 m] long)
• Machete or tomahawk
• Sharpening tool

Steps:
1. Select a dead or half-dead branch of hardwood, approximately 4" to 6" [10 to 15 cm] thick.
2. Choose a side branch that is between 5' to 6' [1.5 to 1.8 m] long and has an 1½" [3.5 cm] thick branch coming off it at a fairly wide angle.
3. Trim the side branch so that it is smooth and even.
4. Use your machete or tomahawk to trim the main branch into a "hook" shape for the handle part.
5. Sharpen the digging end of the branch to a chisel edge.
6. Optionally, fire harden the digging end for increased durability and efficiency.

Fishing Traps (7) -

Construct Arrowhead Tidal Fish Trap
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Wire netting
• Rope or twine
• Stones or weights

Steps:
1. Select the Site: Choose an estuary or sheltered cove with a beach that slopes evenly and has tides between 4' to 6' [1.2 to 1.8 m].
2. **Install Central...
Materials Needed:
• Wire netting
• Rope or twine
• Stones or weights

Steps:
1. Select the Site: Choose an estuary or sheltered cove with a beach that slopes evenly and has tides between 4' to 6' [1.2 to 1.8 m].
2. Install Central Fence: Run a fence of wire netting out from the shore so that the top of the fence is a few inches above high water level, and the lower end has a foot to eighteen inches of water at low tide.
3. Add Wing Fences: From the low water end of the central fence, run two wing fences each at an angle of about 45°. These should extend halfway up to the high-level water mark.
4. Install Parallel Fences: From the shore end of the wing fences, run two short fences parallel to the beach and stop them with a turnback to the arrowhead about two yards [metres] short of the central fence.

How It Works:
• Fish swim into the trap during rising tides and are guided by the structure to the deep water end. They are turned back by the wing fences and parallel fences, trapping them in a pool until low tide.
• At low tide, you can collect only the fish needed, leaving others to escape at the next high tide.

Advantages:
• Catches only large fish.
• Does not kill non-target species.
• Provides a regular supply of food.
arrowhead tidal fish trap, fishing traps, survival fishing, bushcraft fishing
Construct Crab or Lobster Net
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Circular wire hoop (3' to 4' [90 to 120 cm] in diameter)
• Netting or thin bagging
• Rope
• Weight (stone)
• Bait (fish heads, small fish portions)

Steps:
1. Create the Hoop: Make a circular wire hoop with a diameter...
Materials Needed:
• Circular wire hoop (3' to 4' [90 to 120 cm] in diameter)
• Netting or thin bagging
• Rope
• Weight (stone)
• Bait (fish heads, small fish portions)

Steps:
1. Create the Hoop: Make a circular wire hoop with a diameter of 3' to 4' [90 to 120 cm].
2. Attach Netting: Sew a piece of net or thin bagging around the edges of the hoop, allowing for about 1' [30 cm] sag.
3. Add Rope Attachments: Tie three or four short lengths of rope to the wire hoop and join them together about 3' [90 cm] above the hoop.
4. Attach Hoisting Rope: Connect these cords from the hoop to a hoisting rope, which can be buoyed or tied to a post or rock depending on location.
5. Weight and Bait: Weight the bottom of the net with a piece of rock and bait it with fish heads or portions of small fish. Securely tie the bait to the bottom of the net.
6. Deploy Net: Lower the net into the sea and leave it for about two hours.
7. Retrieve Net: Pull the net up swiftly, and any crabs or lobsters feeding on the bait will be caught in the sag.

Placement Tips:
• For lobsters: Set the net on a rocky weedy bottom.
• For crabs: Set it on a sandy bottom, preferably near an underwater reef.
crab lobster net, fishing traps, survival fishing, bushcraft fishing
Construct Tidal Rock Pool Trap
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Rocks
• Bait (crushed shellfish, small fish, rock crabs)

Steps:
1. Select the Site: Choose an area with multiple rock pools that are well covered at high tide and barely dry at low tide.
2. Bait the Rock Pool:...
Materials Needed:
• Rocks
• Bait (crushed shellfish, small fish, rock crabs)

Steps:
1. Select the Site: Choose an area with multiple rock pools that are well covered at high tide and barely dry at low tide.
2. Bait the Rock Pool: Select one rock pool and heavily bait it with crushed shellfish, small portions of freshly killed fish, or crushed rock crabs.
3. Build a Wall: Construct a wall of rocks across the normal opening of the selected rock pool so that the top of the wall is a few inches below the water at high tide.
4. Timing: Set up the trap during times when there is a low tide at dusk and dawn to maximize fish attraction.

How It Works:
• Fish are attracted to the baited rock pool during the night as the tide rises.
• As the tide falls, they become trapped in the pool until the next high tide.
• At low tide, you can collect your catch using a scoop net.

Advantages:
• Requires minimal materials and effort.
• Effective for catching fish in rocky coastal areas.
tidal rock pool trap, fishing traps, survival fishing, bushcraft fishing
Construct a Drummet Fish Trap
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Wire
• Netting or mesh
• Stones (for weighting)
• Bait (fish heads, shellfish, etc.)

Steps:
1. Create the Structure: Make a wire cage with inverted cone-shaped entrances at both ends. The cones should lead inward to...
Materials Needed:
• Wire
• Netting or mesh
• Stones (for weighting)
• Bait (fish heads, shellfish, etc.)

Steps:
1. Create the Structure: Make a wire cage with inverted cone-shaped entrances at both ends. The cones should lead inward to trap fish.
2. Install Mesh: Cover the cage with netting or mesh of large size so that small fish can escape freely.
3. Weight the Bottom: Add a couple of heavy stones to the bottom of the drummet for stability.
4. Bait the Trap: Place bait inside the trap, such as fish heads, inedible fish, or shellfish, to attract fish.
5. Deploy the Drummet: Set the trap in mid-stream, drop it into a deep pool near a river, or place it off a rocky ledge at the seaside.

How It Works:
• Fish are attracted to the bait and swim through the inverted cone-shaped entrances, which prevent them from escaping once inside.
• The trap ensures a regular supply of fresh fish when checked periodically.

Advantages:
• Simple design that can be set in various environments (rivers, seas).
• Effective for long-term use with minimal maintenance.
drummet fish trap, fishing traps, survival fishing, bushcraft fishing
Constructing a Lobster or Craypot
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Board (1' [30 cm] square by 1" [2.5 cm] thick)
• ¼" [6 mm] diameter holes
• Cane (5' [1.5 m] lengths)
• Heavy stone for weighting
• Bait (old fish heads or other fish bait)
• Rope with buoy for marking position

**Steps to...
Materials Required:
• Board (1' [30 cm] square by 1" [2.5 cm] thick)
• ¼" [6 mm] diameter holes
• Cane (5' [1.5 m] lengths)
• Heavy stone for weighting
• Bait (old fish heads or other fish bait)
• Rope with buoy for marking position

Steps to Construct the Pot:
1. Prepare the Board:
- Draw a circle on one side of the board with a diameter of about 8" [20 cm].
- Drill ¼" [6 mm] holes around the circle, spaced about 1" [2.5 cm] apart.

2. Insert and Weave Cane:
- Insert 5' [1.5 m] lengths of cane into each hole.
- Begin weaving split cane above the board to form a wide funnel shape.
- Gradually bend the upright canes as you weave until they come over and down.

3. Shape the Pot:
- Turn the structure upside down for convenience during construction.
- At the base (about 2' [60 cm] from the top), turn the canes sharply inward toward the center of the circle.

4. Complete and Deploy:
- When turned right side up, this forms the bottom of the trap.
- Weight the bottom with a heavy stone.
- Bait with old fish heads or other suitable bait.
- Lower the lobster pot into a rocky weedy position where lobsters are likely to live in colonies.

Notes:
• The hoisting rope must be buoyed with a marker for easy retrieval.
• It may take several days for the trap to 'weather' and attract lobsters or crayfish.
• Multiple pots can provide a steady supply of seafood.
lobster pot,craypot,cane weaving,fish bait,rocky weedy position
Constructing a Lobster or Yabbie Snare
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Heavy gauge wire (8 g.)
• Cross wires
• Heavy nylon fishing line
• Bait
• Cords and central rope
• Buoy

Steps to Construct the Snare:
1. Create a Circle of Wire:
- Make a circle of heavy gauge wire with a...
Materials Required:
• Heavy gauge wire (8 g.)
• Cross wires
• Heavy nylon fishing line
• Bait
• Cords and central rope
• Buoy

Steps to Construct the Snare:
1. Create a Circle of Wire:
- Make a circle of heavy gauge wire with a diameter between 12" to 15" [30 to 37.5 cm].
- Secure two cross wires to support the shape.

2. Add Running Nooses:
- Around the circle, tie a series of running nooses using heavy nylon fishing line.
- Each noose should be no more than 2" [5 cm] across.
- Space the nooses about 1" to 1½" [2.5 to 4 cm] apart around the circle.

3. Attach Bait:
- Tie bait in the center where the cross wires intersect.

4. Secure with Cords and Buoy:
- Attach three or four cords from the circle to a central rope.
- Buoy the central rope to mark its position in water.

Notes:
• This snare is excellent for catching lobsters or yabbies.
• Ensure that the buoy is visible and secure.

Creating a Hollow Log Trap for Freshwater Fish
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Hollow log (not too large in diameter)
• Wire netting or other water-permeable material
• Rope or sling
• Bait

Steps to Construct the Trap:
1. Prepare the Log:
- Cover one end of the hollow log with wire netting...
Materials Required:
• Hollow log (not too large in diameter)
• Wire netting or other water-permeable material
• Rope or sling
• Bait

Steps to Construct the Trap:
1. Prepare the Log:
- Cover one end of the hollow log with wire netting or other material that allows free flow of water.

2. Create a Sling:
- Make a sling so that when the rope is pulled, it tilts the log to position the closed end lowest.

3. Bait and Deployment:
- Place bait a few inches from the closed end inside the log.
- Lower the trap into a convenient pool or off a rock ledge.

Key Considerations:
• The open end of the hollow log should always be upstream to prevent fish from being washed out by current.
• Fish will swim into the log, unable to turn around and escape.
• A similar method can use an open-necked pickle bottle with bait at the lower end. Ensure all air is removed before placing it in shallow water.
• This trap is effective for catching small fish such as sand mullet or whiting for use as bait.
hollow log trap,freshwater fish,wire netting,bait,pickle bottle

Tanning Hides (7) -

Braining a Hide

Braining a Hide

Braining a Hide

Overview: Braining involves applying the brain of the animal to the hide as a lubricant and temporary water repellant.

Steps:
1. Soak the hide on the stretcher.
2. Extract the brain from the animal.
3. Mix the brains with water to create a pasty solution.
4. Once the brains are warm and thoroughly mixed, rub the mixture into the hide. Rub firmly into the hide with your hand on only the hairless side.
braining hide, tanning process, brain application

Fleshing a Hide

Overview: Fleshing is the process of removing meat, tissue, and fat from a hide. It should be done as soon as possible, preferably before the hide starts to dry.

Steps:
1. Soak or wet the hide if it is dry.
2. Lay the hide on a solid, smooth, round object, such as a log or canteen.
3. Hold a bayonet, blunt knife, sharp stone, or bone tool scrapper at a 10-degree angle away from your body. Push the fat and membrane off the leather. Be careful not to make holes in the more tender parts of the belly. For beaver and badger hides, the fat must be cut off.
4. Continue this process until all the fat is completely removed.
fleshing hide, remove fat from hide, tanning process
Removing Hair from a Hide
Standard Source: USMC Summer Survival Course Handbook
Removing Hair from a Hide

Overview: After fleshing and stretching the hide, the hair must be removed.

Steps:
1. Use a sharp stone tool scrapper to scrape off the hair.
2. Soaking the hide in water will make this process easier.
scraping hide hair, tanning process
Rubbing a Hide

Rubbing a Hide

Overview: Rubbing is a critical step to create heat and finish the drying, stretching, and breaking of the grain.

Methods:
1. Cut the skin from the frame around the perimeter, leaving only the lacing holes and hair...
Rubbing a Hide

Overview: Rubbing is a critical step to create heat and finish the drying, stretching, and breaking of the grain.

Methods:
1. Cut the skin from the frame around the perimeter, leaving only the lacing holes and hair that could not be removed.
2. Use a one-half inch rope attached between two trees. Grasp the skin at different points all around its perimeter and pull, pull, pull.
3. If rope is not available, rub the skin by sitting on the ground and hooking the skin over your feet and pulling.
rubbing hide, tanning process, friction rubbing

Smoking a Hide

Overview: Smoking the hide helps make it water repellant.

Steps:
1. Add wet or green wood chips to the fire. Sage or willow are good woods. The objective is to get the chips to smoke, not burn.
2. It only takes a few minutes to smoke, but be careful to prevent flame from ruining the hide.
smoking hide, tanning process, wood chips

1. Frame stretching: Lace the hide to a frame with cordage and pull it tight.
2. **Ground...
Stretching a Hide

Overview: A fresh green or soaked hide must be stretched to prevent it from shrinking and hardening.

Methods of Stretching:
1. Frame stretching: Lace the hide to a frame with cordage and pull it tight.
2. Ground stretching: Stake the hide tightly to the ground.
3. Create a frame by bending a stick back on itself.
4. The less a hide shrinks and hardens, the softer it will be at the end of the process.
stretching hide, tanning process, frame stretching

Uses for Animal Hides

Overview: The uses of animal hides are limited only by the imagination. Below are a few ideas:
• Making clothing or footwear.
• Creating shelter materials (e.g., tents, blankets).
• Crafting tools and containers.
• Using as insulation in shelters.
• Making ropes or cords from the hide fibers.
animal hide uses, tanning applications, survival materials

Materials Needed: Egg, fire.

Steps:
1. Pierce the shell and inner membrane of the egg to allow steam to escape.
2. Place the egg in the hot ashes of your fire.
3. Allow it to bake until fully cooked.

Notes: Failing to pierce the egg may cause it to explode due to trapped steam.
egg baking,bushcraft cooking,hot ashes

1. Wrap the food in clay or damp paper and bury it in hot ashes beneath your campfire.
2. Leave the food for six to eight hours; it will not spoil or overcook.
3. This method...
Materials Needed: Clay or damp paper, food to be cooked.

Steps:
1. Wrap the food in clay or damp paper and bury it in hot ashes beneath your campfire.
2. Leave the food for six to eight hours; it will not spoil or overcook.
3. This method is ideal for cooking freshly-killed meat, fish, or game without needing to skin or pluck them first.

Notes: The intestines and outer covering (fur/feathers) will shrink and peel away when unwrapped.
bury food cooking,bushcraft cooking,slow cooking
Create a Stone Oven for Baking
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed: Stones, fire, food to be baked.

Steps:
1. Create a stone oven by digging a hole and lining it with stones.
2. Light a fire inside the oven until the stones are sizzling hot.
3. Extinguish the fire and place your food...
Materials Needed: Stones, fire, food to be baked.

Steps:
1. Create a stone oven by digging a hole and lining it with stones.
2. Light a fire inside the oven until the stones are sizzling hot.
3. Extinguish the fire and place your food (e.g., meat or scone) into the heated cavity.
4. Cover the oven with dirt removed from the hole to retain heat.

Notes: The temperature will gradually decrease, preventing burning. This method is ideal for baking without direct flame contact.
stone oven,baking in the wild,bushcraft cooking

Steps:
1. Light the hollow inside of a log or stump with fire.
2. Place your food (covered on top and underneath) inside the hollow.
3. Monitor the fire closely; if it becomes too...
Materials Needed: Hollow log or stump, food to be baked.

Steps:
1. Light the hollow inside of a log or stump with fire.
2. Place your food (covered on top and underneath) inside the hollow.
3. Monitor the fire closely; if it becomes too fierce, dampen it with water splashes.

Notes: This method is quick but requires constant attention to prevent burning.
hollow log oven,bushcraft cooking,stump baking

Materials Needed: Forked stick with sharpened ends, flat stone, fire.

Steps:
1. Use a forked stick with sharpened ends to grill meat directly over the fire.
2. Alternatively, heat a flat stone in the fire until it is nearly red hot.
3. Remove the stone from the fire and dust it clean.
4. Place the meat on top of the hot stone for grilling.

Notes: This method allows for even cooking without direct flame contact.
grill meat,bushcraft cooking,hot stone grilling,forked stick
Use a Dug Hole with Stones for Cooking
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed: Shovel or digging tool, stones, food to be cooked.

Steps:
1. Dig a hole and line it with stones.
2. Light a quick fire inside the hole until the stones are thoroughly heated.
3. Extinguish the fire, leaving only hot white...
Materials Needed: Shovel or digging tool, stones, food to be cooked.

Steps:
1. Dig a hole and line it with stones.
2. Light a quick fire inside the hole until the stones are thoroughly heated.
3. Extinguish the fire, leaving only hot white ash behind.
4. Wrap your food in clay or damp paper and place it on top of the heated stones.
5. Cover the hole with the dirt removed during digging to retain heat.

Notes: This method is similar to burying food in ashes but uses stones for more even heating.
dug hole cooking,bushcraft cooking,stone heat method

Animal Tracks (6) -

Identify Animal Tracks by Digging Claws
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Examine the animal track for prominent claws on the center toes.
2. Deduce that these are the tracks of an earth digger or burrower.
3. Note that the prehensile thumb is undeveloped, which confirms this...
Procedure:

1. Examine the animal track for prominent claws on the center toes.
2. Deduce that these are the tracks of an earth digger or burrower.
3. Note that the prehensile thumb is undeveloped, which confirms this classification.
4. Recognize that digging claws may appear on fore or hind feet, but they are most prominent on the fore feet.
5. Understand that these claws are an invariable mark of a digger, regardless of whether they appear on front or hind feet.

Details:
• Digging claws are used for burrowing and digging.
• These tracks are typically made by animals such as moles, badgers, or other subterranean creatures.
digging claws,burrower tracks
Identify Animal Tracks by Prehensile Thumbs
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Examine the animal track for the presence of a prehensile thumb, which is a single or double digit.
2. Recognize that this feature indicates the animal is a true tree climber.
3. Compare to your own hand and note that...
Procedure:

1. Examine the animal track for the presence of a prehensile thumb, which is a single or double digit.
2. Recognize that this feature indicates the animal is a true tree climber.
3. Compare to your own hand and note that humans cannot climb trees like monkeys or other true climbers.
4. Note exceptions, such as the tree climbing kangaroo, which uses a prehensile tail instead of a thumb for climbing.
5. The domestic cat is not considered a true tree climber despite its claws; it can only 'claw' its way up, not truly climb like a monkey.

Details:
• Prehensile thumbs are the defining feature of tree climbers.
• Use this to distinguish between tree climbers and other animals.
animal tracks,prehensile thumb,tree climbing
Identify Animal Tracks by Pronounced Toes
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Examine the track for pronounced toes, which are not used for climbing or digging.
2. Deduce that these tracks belong to a fast-running animal (not a climber or digger).
3. Recognize that grazing animals such as cows,...
Procedure:

1. Examine the track for pronounced toes, which are not used for climbing or digging.
2. Deduce that these tracks belong to a fast-running animal (not a climber or digger).
3. Recognize that grazing animals such as cows, horses, and sheep have hooves, which are the 'tips' of toes or 'toenails'.
4. Understand that fast runners use their pronounced toes for springing action when running.
5. Note that these tracks do not show prehensile thumbs or digging claws.

Details:
• Pronounced toes indicate speed and are used by animals that rely on running to escape predators.
• Grazing animals have hooves instead of pronounced toes.
pronounced toes,fast runners
Identify Animal Trails and Roads in the Bush
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Look for repeated trails or paths that animals use to travel between resting places, feeding grounds, and waterholes.
2. Recognize these as 'roads' of bush creatures, which are used continuously by various animals.
3. Observe...
Procedure:

1. Look for repeated trails or paths that animals use to travel between resting places, feeding grounds, and waterholes.
2. Recognize these as 'roads' of bush creatures, which are used continuously by various animals.
3. Observe the number and newness of tracks and droppings on these trails to gauge animal traffic volume.
4. Understand that placing an obstacle on a trail will cause animals to make a detour but return once the obstacle is removed.
5. Use this knowledge to predict where animals are likely to travel or rest.

Details:
• These trails are often well-worn and visible even in dense vegetation.
• They may be used by multiple species depending on the terrain and resources available.
animal trails,bush roads
Identify Tree Roads Used by Possums and Koalas
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Examine the upward side of a leaning gum tree for scratch marks.
2. Recognize that these are signs of a 'tree road' used by possums or koalas.
3. Look for scratch marks of varying ages, which indicate frequent use of the...
Procedure:

1. Examine the upward side of a leaning gum tree for scratch marks.
2. Recognize that these are signs of a 'tree road' used by possums or koalas.
3. Look for scratch marks of varying ages, which indicate frequent use of the tree by animals.
4. Determine if the tree is a feeding tree (showing young leaves or mistletoe berries) or a living-quarters-tree (with many dead, hollow limbs).
5. Use this information to locate where possums and koalas may rest or feed.

Details:
• Possums and koalas use these trees for feeding or sheltering in dead hollows.
• These trees are often marked by repeated climbing activity.
tree roads,possum tracks,koala tracks
Interpret Animal Tracks to Determine Habits
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Observe the tracks and droppings on animal trails to determine the frequency of use.
2. Recognize that animals are creatures of habit, often following the same path daily for water, food, or shelter.
3. Use this knowledge to...
Procedure:

1. Observe the tracks and droppings on animal trails to determine the frequency of use.
2. Recognize that animals are creatures of habit, often following the same path daily for water, food, or shelter.
3. Use this knowledge to predict an animal's movements and behavior in its habitat.
4. Note that changes such as fire, flood, or drought may temporarily alter these patterns but not eliminate them entirely.
5. Understand that animals will return to their usual trails once conditions improve.

Details:
• Animals follow consistent routes for survival purposes.
• This predictability is useful for trappers and observers in the bush.
animal habits,tracking habits

Trapping Methods (6) -

Construct a Falling Cage with Figure 4 Release for Trapping Birds
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Falling Cage Construction with Figure 4 Release

1. Construct a cage using sticks lashed to form a pyramidal or suitable shape, boxwood, or netting, ensuring it is of adequate size.
2. Use the Figure 4 release mechanism for trap activation.
3....
Falling Cage Construction with Figure 4 Release

1. Construct a cage using sticks lashed to form a pyramidal or suitable shape, boxwood, or netting, ensuring it is of adequate size.
2. Use the Figure 4 release mechanism for trap activation.
3. This trap is ideal for ground feeding birds and can be certain for pigeons when baited with grain or small fruits.
4. Cut an upright stick with a chisel edge at the top and square its bottom end a few inches from the base on all four sides.
5. Sharpen a support stick to a chisel edge at one end, and cut a nick parallel to the chisel edge where it will cross the top of the upright.
6. Create a bait stick with an undercut nick at the thickest end and a square face cut at the farthest end from the undercut nick where it crosses the upright.
7. Set the trap by standing the upright in front of the cage, placing the support stick with its nick on the chisel edge of the upright, and ensuring the upper end supports the raised edge of the box.
8. Insert the chisel end of the support stick into the undercut nick at the end of the bait stick.
9. Ensure the squared cut in the bait stick engages with the square face of the support stick, and position the baited end well under the trap.
falling cage, figure 4 release, bird trapping, chisel edge, upright stick
Construct a Log Roofed Pen for Trapping Animals
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Log Roofed Pen Construction

1. Build a pen of adequate size for the animal to be trapped, ensuring it is strongly constructed.
2. Construct the pen with two sides and one end only.
3. Across the closed end, secure a strong cross bar.
4. Use a...
Log Roofed Pen Construction

1. Build a pen of adequate size for the animal to be trapped, ensuring it is strongly constructed.
2. Construct the pen with two sides and one end only.
3. Across the closed end, secure a strong cross bar.
4. Use a toggle and bait stick mechanism similar to the logfall release to control the roof.
5. Place a forked stick upright a few inches from one side of the trap at the open end.
6. Rest the end of the roof logs on a supporting stick placed across the fork.
7. Fasten a length of cord to the far end of the supporting stick and tie a short toggle stick to the end of this cord.
8. Press the end of the toggle stick against the bait stick, which is pressed against stakes opposite and at the far end of the pen.
9. Disturbance of the bait stick releases its engagement with the toggle stick, causing the support stick to release and the roof to fall heavily, imprisoning the animal in the pen.
log roofed pen, trapping animals, toggle mechanism, bait stick, cross bar
Construct a Wire Cage Trap for Rabbits
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Wire Cage Trap Construction for Rabbits

1. Select a suitable burrow in the warren to set up the trap, and cover all other burrows with a layer of paper stuffed into the hole and packed with earth for a few inches.
2. Construct a long cage from...
Wire Cage Trap Construction for Rabbits

1. Select a suitable burrow in the warren to set up the trap, and cover all other burrows with a layer of paper stuffed into the hole and packed with earth for a few inches.
2. Construct a long cage from wire netting with one end closed and the other end as a wire door suspended from the top of the cage.
3. Ensure the wire door can be pushed easily into the trap but cannot be pushed outward once it falls.
4. Place the trap at the entrance to the selected burrow.
5. Rabbits disturbed by the paper in other burrows will eventually approach the burrow with the wire cage.
6. They will push forward into the opening, and the door lifting inward allows them to enter the cage.
7. Once inside, the door drops behind them, preventing escape back into the burrow.
8. This method can capture ten or twelve rabbits per night and is more humane than steel-jawed commercial traps.
wire cage trap, rabbit trapping, warren burrow, humane trapping, wire door
Destroy Snare Sticks Immediately
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Identify Snare Sticks: Look for sticks or devices that have snares attached to them, typically placed in bushes or trees where small birds may frequent.
2. Destroy the Snare Stick: If you find a snare stick, destroy it...
Procedure:

1. Identify Snare Sticks: Look for sticks or devices that have snares attached to them, typically placed in bushes or trees where small birds may frequent.
2. Destroy the Snare Stick: If you find a snare stick, destroy it immediately without hesitation. This includes cutting the stick and removing any remaining parts of the snare.
3. Do Not Consider Feelings of the Person Who Placed It: Regardless of who placed the snare or why, destroy it to prevent harm to wildlife.
4. Report Illegal Trapping Activities: If you find evidence of illegal trapping methods (e.g., snares that are not for legitimate purposes), consider reporting this to local authorities or conservation groups.
snare sticks, destroy snare, illegal trapping, wildlife protection
Humanely Trap Animals Using Pen or Box Traps
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Select Appropriate Traps: Use pen or box traps designed to capture animals alive without causing discomfort or pain.
2. Set the Trap Correctly: Ensure that the trap is placed in a location where the target animal is likely...
Procedure:

1. Select Appropriate Traps: Use pen or box traps designed to capture animals alive without causing discomfort or pain.
2. Set the Trap Correctly: Ensure that the trap is placed in a location where the target animal is likely to be found, such as near feeding areas or along travel routes.
3. Use Bait: Place bait inside the trap that is attractive to the specific type of animal you are trying to capture (e.g., meat for carnivores, seeds for small mammals).
4. Check the Trap Regularly: Never leave a set trap unattended for more than a few hours. Check it frequently to ensure that any captured animals are released or handled promptly.
5. Handle Captured Animals with Care: Once an animal is caught, handle it gently and release it in a safe location if it is not being kept for a specific purpose (e.g., relocation or population control).
6. Avoid Mechanical Traps: Avoid using mechanical traps such as rabbit traps that cause injury or prolonged suffering to the captured animal.
7. Use Logfalls for Instant Kill: If instant kill is necessary, use logfall traps which kill the animal quickly and humanely when it touches the bait.
humane trapping, pen trap, box trap, mechanical traps, logfalls, animal capture
Reasons for Trapping Wild Animals and Birds
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Understand the Purpose of Trapping: Trapping is justified primarily on grounds of 'Preservation', which includes protecting native wildlife from predators or other threats.
2. Control Predatory Species: Trap wild animals...
Procedure:

1. Understand the Purpose of Trapping: Trapping is justified primarily on grounds of 'Preservation', which includes protecting native wildlife from predators or other threats.
2. Control Predatory Species: Trap wild animals such as cats, dogs, foxes, and wild dogs that have gone bush and are known to prey on other species. These animals can be particularly destructive to native wildlife populations.
3. Use Trapping for Population Control: In areas where certain species overpopulate or threaten the balance of an ecosystem, trapping may be necessary to maintain ecological harmony.
4. Avoid Unnecessary Trapping: Only trap when it is absolutely necessary and ensure that all trapping methods are humane and do not cause unnecessary suffering to animals.
5. Educate Others About Responsible Trapping: Share knowledge about responsible trapping practices with others, especially young people who may be interested in learning survival or bushcraft skills.
reasons for trapping, preservation, predator control, population management

Trapping (6) -

Prepare and Bait Traps Correctly
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Choose the Right Location:
- Select a site based on animal activity patterns, not just visual appeal.
- Animals rely more on scent than sight to detect danger.

2. Remove Human Scent from Traps:
- Drown human...
Procedure:

1. Choose the Right Location:
- Select a site based on animal activity patterns, not just visual appeal.
- Animals rely more on scent than sight to detect danger.

2. Remove Human Scent from Traps:
- Drown human scent by using stronger lures such as oil of aniseed or oil of rhodium.
- Scorch the trap to burn off scent.
- Allow the trap to weather and lose its scent over time.

3. Test Bait Effectiveness:
- Set up a test area on clean, dusty ground.
- Drive 15-20 small stakes into the ground and attach different baits (some with lures, some without).
- Mark each stake with a number and note which bait is attached to it.

4. Check Results:
- Visit the test area the next morning to observe tracks left by animals that visited during the night.
- This will indicate which baits are effective for the local wildlife.

5. Set Traps with Correct Bait:
- Use the information from your test to set traps with appropriate baits.
- Tree-climbing animals prefer fruits.
- Digging animals prefer sweet potato, carrot, or other edible roots.
- Flesh-eating animals will take flesh as bait.
- Herbivores may be attracted to cabbage, lettuce, or bread.
trap preparation, baiting traps, scent removal, testing baits
Construct a Crossbar Bait Snare
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
1. Cut two stout straight stakes.
2. On the upper end of each stake, cut a nick with the straight step on the top end of the stake.
3. Cut a cross bar with a side branch so that the end of the side branch is a few inches away from the cross bar.
4....
1. Cut two stout straight stakes.
2. On the upper end of each stake, cut a nick with the straight step on the top end of the stake.
3. Cut a cross bar with a side branch so that the end of the side branch is a few inches away from the cross bar.
4. Square the side farthest away from the side branch on top and sides to fit the squared faces of the stakes.
5. Lop a whippy sapling of its branches and top.
6. Bend the sapling over so that it comes directly above the head of the two stakes driven into the ground.
7. Tie a cord from the head of the sapling to the centre of the cross bar.
8. Bait the side branch.
9. Spread two nooses either side of the baited crossbar.
crossbar,bait,snare,trapping,bushcraft
Construct a Double Spring Snare
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
1. Trim two saplings and bend them towards each other.
2. Tie two interlocking sticks at their heads.
3. Cut the interlocking sticks so that they step into each other.
4. Lash a bait stick to one of the interlocking sticks.
5. Lash four cords for...
1. Trim two saplings and bend them towards each other.
2. Tie two interlocking sticks at their heads.
3. Cut the interlocking sticks so that they step into each other.
4. Lash a bait stick to one of the interlocking sticks.
5. Lash four cords for nooses (two onto each sapling head).
6. Set the snare by bending the two saplings over, locking the two sticks together.
7. Set the nooses, two on each side of the bait, and a few inches distant.
8. When an animal disturbs the bait, it twists the interlocking sticks, releasing the two saplings.
9. The saplings spring apart, pulling the nooses against each other to securely hold the captured animal.
double spring,snare,trapping,bushcraft
Construct a Double-ended Figure Four Snare
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
1. Trim a whippy sapling of its branches and head.
2. Bend the sapling over and mark the site directly beneath the head on the ground.
3. Tie a stout cord to the head of the sapling before releasing it.
4. Cut three sticks for the release: one is a...
1. Trim a whippy sapling of its branches and head.
2. Bend the sapling over and mark the site directly beneath the head on the ground.
3. Tie a stout cord to the head of the sapling before releasing it.
4. Cut three sticks for the release: one is a stake that must be sharpened at the point and bevelled at the head.
5. About 8" [20 cm] from the head, square off two sides at right angles to each other on the stake.
6. About 3" [7.5 cm] below the head, make an undercut nick in one of the sides opposite to one of the squared sides.
7. Cut a crossbar bait stick that is 2' [60 cm] long.
8. Nick or cut the centre of the crossbar bait stick to provide a squared step less than ¼" deep.
9. At the end farthest from this step, make an undercut nick at right angles to it.
10. Cut a toggle release stick with one end sharpened to a chisel edge and place it in the undercut nick of the stake.
11. Place the crossbar with its step against the squared edge of the stake, with the undercut nick facing upwards.
12. Mark on the toggle stick where the end should be cut to sit in the upper nick of the crossbar.
13. Sharpen the toggle stick to a chisel point.
14. Tie the free end of the cord from the sapling head to the toggle stick.
15. Tie four nooses on cords and set them in position with forked twigs.
double-ended,figure four,snare,trapping,bushcraft
Construct a Portable Box Trap with Internal Release Mechanism
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• A stout box suitable for the target animal
• Sliding door (with grooves for smooth movement)
• Stiff wire (8 gauge)
• Bait
• Staple

Steps to Construct:
1. Box Construction: Make a sturdy box of a size appropriate...
Materials Required:
• A stout box suitable for the target animal
• Sliding door (with grooves for smooth movement)
• Stiff wire (8 gauge)
• Bait
• Staple

Steps to Construct:
1. Box Construction: Make a sturdy box of a size appropriate for the animal you intend to trap.
2. Sliding Door Installation: Fit a sliding door on one end, ensuring it slides easily between two grooves.
3. Drill Hole in Door: Bore a small hole (about ¼" deep) near the lower end of the inside of the door.
4. Drill Hole in Roof: On the roof of the box, bore a hole about 1" [2.5 cm] diameter through the wood, approximately 3" [7.5 cm] from the closed-in end.
5. Release Mechanism Setup:
- Take a piece of stiff wire (8 gauge) and bend an eye at the head.
- Bend another eye about 6" [15 cm] lower down, immediately below this lower eye, and shape the wire into a wide hook before cutting it off at the end of the hook.
- Pass another short piece of wire through the top eye (with the eye in the center of the hole in the roof), and secure it parallel to the end of the box with staples at either end.
6. Internal Wire Installation: Fasten another piece of wire to the lower eye inside the box, ensuring its length allows the hook to engage in the hole bored in the door when slightly forward.
7. Bait Setup: Secure the bait to the U-shaped hook on the lower end of the internal wire.
8. Final Adjustment: Place the free end of the inner piece of wire inside the hole at the lower end of the door.

How It Works:
• When the animal disturbs the bait, the wire holding up the door is withdrawn, causing the door to drop and imprison the animal.
box trap, internal release, sliding door, wire mechanism, trapping, survival, bushcraft
Construct a Track Spring Snare
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
1. Select a site on an animal trail where a tall sapling is available a few feet to one side of the track.
2. Lop the sapling of its branches and top.
3. Tie a stout cord to the head of the sapling.
4. Drive tall stout pegs well into the ground on...
1. Select a site on an animal trail where a tall sapling is available a few feet to one side of the track.
2. Lop the sapling of its branches and top.
3. Tie a stout cord to the head of the sapling.
4. Drive tall stout pegs well into the ground on either side of the track where the bent sapling crosses the trail.
5. Securely lash a cross bar to the tops of these stakes (or use convenient trees instead of stakes).
6. Tie a stout cord or rope to the head of the sapling and tie a thin strong stick a few feet along the cord.
7. The stick should nearly reach from the crossbar to the ground.
8. Tie the cord from the sapling a few inches below one end of the stick.
9. Place this end under the crossbar, with the lower end laid against a thin cross-stick.
10. Lightly tie the noose of the snare to the top crossbar and stakes to keep it spread open.
11. Release is triggered when the animal touches either the bottom stick (knocking it down) or the toggle stick with the cord, releasing the sapling and tightening the noose around the animal's neck.
track spring,snare,trapping,bushcraft

Camping Tools (5) -

Creating a Candle Holder from a Bottle
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Clear glass bottle
• Thin wire or grease-soaked string
• Cold water

Steps:
1. Cut the Bottle: To cut the base of a clear glass bottle cleanly, heat a piece of thin wire to red heat.
2. Bend Wire Around Bottle:...
Materials Required:
• Clear glass bottle
• Thin wire or grease-soaked string
• Cold water

Steps:
1. Cut the Bottle: To cut the base of a clear glass bottle cleanly, heat a piece of thin wire to red heat.
2. Bend Wire Around Bottle: Bend this around the bottle where you want to cut it (alternatively, tie a piece of grease-soaked string round the bottle and burn it).
3. Immerse in Cold Water: When the hot wire or burning string is around the bottle, immerse the bottle in cold water.
4. Break Off Base: The glass will break off evenly at the place where the wire or string encircled it.
5. Use as Candle Holder or Lamp Cover: This can be used as a candle holder or glass cover for a slush lamp.
candle holder,bottle,cutting,glass,bushcraft
Making a Slush Lamp
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Old tin or small hollow piece of branch
• Clayey earth
• Twig
• Cotton rag or finely teased bark fibre (for wick)
• Cooking fat

Steps:
1. Fill Container with Earth: Fill an old tin or small hollow piece of branch...
Materials Required:
• Old tin or small hollow piece of branch
• Clayey earth
• Twig
• Cotton rag or finely teased bark fibre (for wick)
• Cooking fat

Steps:
1. Fill Container with Earth: Fill an old tin or small hollow piece of branch with clayey earth, packed tight at the bottom.
2. Leave Space for Fat and Wick: The earth should come to about 1" [2.5 cm] from the top of the tin.
3. Insert Twig: Into this, push a twig.
4. Create Wick: Wind a piece of old cotton rag or very finely teased bark fibre around the twig to serve as a wick.
5. Add Fat: Pour cooking fat on top of the earth.
6. Light the Lamp: When the wick is lit, the lamp burns with a clear flame.
7. Control Light Output: The amount of light can be controlled by adjusting the size of the wick.
slush lamp,bushcraft,camping,light,fat,wick
Constructing a Bush Ladder
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Two long, straight poles of equal length
• Rungs (straight sticks)
• Lashing material (e.g., rope or natural fibers)

Steps to Construct the Bush Ladder:
1. Select and Prepare Poles:
- Choose two long, straight...
Materials Required:
• Two long, straight poles of equal length
• Rungs (straight sticks)
• Lashing material (e.g., rope or natural fibers)

Steps to Construct the Bush Ladder:
1. Select and Prepare Poles:
- Choose two long, straight poles and cut them to equal lengths.
2. Lash Thin Ends Together:
- Secure the thin ends of the poles together using lashing material.
3. Spread Thick Ends Apart:
- Spread the thick ends (butts) of the poles so they are approximately 2½' to 3' [75 to 90 cm] apart.
4. Attach Rungs:
- Lash the rungs between the two poles, ensuring that the lashings are tight and secure.
5. Ease Lashing Process with Support:
- To make lashing easier, lift the butts of the poles onto a log or large stones to allow for better access when passing the lashing material under the poles.
bush ladder,bushcraft,camping,tools,ladder
Constructing a Camp Loom
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Two stout forked stakes, about 2" [5 cm] thick
• A cross bar, about 1" [2.5 cm] thick
• A row of straight, smooth stakes, each about 4' [1.2 m] long

Steps to Construct the Camp Loom:
1. **Drive Forked Stakes into...
Materials Required:
• Two stout forked stakes, about 2" [5 cm] thick
• A cross bar, about 1" [2.5 cm] thick
• A row of straight, smooth stakes, each about 4' [1.2 m] long

Steps to Construct the Camp Loom:
1. Drive Forked Stakes into Ground:
- Insert two forked stakes into the ground with their lower prongs approximately 3' [90 cm] above the ground.
- Ensure the prongs are facing away from the direction you wish to work.
2. Position Cross Bar:
- Place a cross bar across the forks of the stakes, ensuring it is trimmed of twigs and roughnesses for smooth weaving.
3. Set Up Row of Stakes:
- Drive a row of straight, smooth stakes into the ground approximately 8' or 9' [2.4 or 2.7 m] from the cross bar on the side farthest from the prongs.
- Ensure there are about 2" [5 cm] between the centres of these stakes and trim them of any side twigs or roughnesses.
4. Position Weaving Bar:
- Cut a weaving bar that is a few inches longer than the width of the row of stakes.
- Lay it on the ground, parallel to the row of stakes and about 6" [15 cm] in front of them.

Alternative Setup Using Trees:
• If two trees are available at a convenient distance apart:
- At ground level and about 7' [2.1 m] above the ground, lash two stout cross bars (2" [5 cm] thick) to the tree trunks.
- Lash a series of smooth vertical sticks between these cross bars, spaced approximately 2" [5 cm] apart at their centres.
camp loom,bushcraft,weaving,tools,camping
Constructing a Camp Mattress or Stick Hammock
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Weft strands (longer strands)
• Tufts of grass, ferns, or sticks (for stick hammocks)

Steps to Construct the Camp Mattress or Stick Hammock:
1. Set Up Weft Strands:
- Set up the weft strands as you would for...
Materials Required:
• Weft strands (longer strands)
• Tufts of grass, ferns, or sticks (for stick hammocks)

Steps to Construct the Camp Mattress or Stick Hammock:
1. Set Up Weft Strands:
- Set up the weft strands as you would for regular weaving.
2. Pass Tufts Between Weft Strands:
- Instead of using warp (cross) strands, pass tufts of grass, ferns, or sticks between the weft strands to create a mattress or stick hammock.
3. Add Warp Ties for Stability:
- When weaving a camp mattress, add a warp tie every second or third lift to bind the sides and prevent the outside weft strands from spreading.

Additional Tips for Weaving a Camp Blanket:
• Use sun-dried glass fibers (if available) loosely spun into a covering for a camp bed in place of blankets.
• Ensure that warp strands are pushed closely together for better insulation.
• It is preferable to make two light grass coverings rather than one heavy blanket, as multiple layers provide more warmth than extreme thickness.
camp mattress,camp blanket,bushcraft,stick hammock,tools,camping

Wild Fishing (5) -

Identify Safe Saltwater Fish for Consumption
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Assess the fish's appearance:
- Check if the fish has the usual 'fish shape' and scales.
- If it has these characteristics, it is generally safe to eat.
2. Identify potentially poisonous fish:
- Avoid eating fish...
Procedure:

1. Assess the fish's appearance:
- Check if the fish has the usual 'fish shape' and scales.
- If it has these characteristics, it is generally safe to eat.
2. Identify potentially poisonous fish:
- Avoid eating fish that lack the conventional fish shape or scales unless you are certain they are safe.
- Examples of potentially poisonous fish include:
- Puffer (toady)
- Box fish
- Pig fish
- The leatherjacket is an exception and may be edible, but avoid its roe (eggs) and liver.
3. Handle with caution:
- Shark flesh is safe to eat, but do not consume shark innards or liver due to their high vitamin D content, which can be fatal if consumed in large quantities.
4. Cooking considerations:
- Eels are edible and have minute scales; they should be properly cooked for best results.
5. Be aware of spoilage:
- Many tropical fish may appear poisonous due to rapid putrefaction after death, not actual poison in the flesh.
- Cook these fish immediately after catching them to avoid poisoning.
6. Avoid unknown species:
- If a fish does not have scales or is of unusual shape, do not eat it unless you are certain it is safe.
saltwater fish, identify edible fish, poisonous fish, shark liver, eel, pufferfish, box fish, leatherjacket
Cook Bivalves for Consumption
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Collect and prepare bivalves:
- Gather a dozen to eighteen bivalves for one person.
- Wash the bivalves thoroughly in water to remove all sand.
2. Cooking process:
- Place the bivalves in a billy (a type of pot)...
Procedure:

1. Collect and prepare bivalves:
- Gather a dozen to eighteen bivalves for one person.
- Wash the bivalves thoroughly in water to remove all sand.
2. Cooking process:
- Place the bivalves in a billy (a type of pot) and pour boiling water over them.
- The shells will open, allowing you to easily remove the flesh from the two halves.
3. Further preparation:
- Cut the flesh into small pieces before cooking.
4. Boiling instructions:
- Boil the bivalves in fresh water for ten minutes.
5. Add ingredients after boiling:
- Add thickening (such as flour or cornstarch) and salt to taste.
6. Optional additions:
- You can add milk or dried milk if desired, especially when making a soup like Pippie soup, which is similar to New Zealand Toheroa soup.
bivalves, cooking bivalves, pippie soup, toheroa soup, boiling shellfish
Identify and Prepare Shellfish for Consumption
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Assess shellfish safety:
- Most bivalve shellfish are safe to eat, except for saltwater mussels during certain periods of the year.
- Avoid eating shellfish from contaminated waters or freshwater sources that may be...
Procedure:

1. Assess shellfish safety:
- Most bivalve shellfish are safe to eat, except for saltwater mussels during certain periods of the year.
- Avoid eating shellfish from contaminated waters or freshwater sources that may be polluted with sewage.
2. Cooking requirements:
- Freshwater shellfish must be well cooked to destroy any possible parasites and their eggs.
3. Tenderizing tough flesh:
- If the shellfish flesh is tough, beat it gently to make it more tender for eating.
4. Cooking methods:
- Shellfish can be prepared by boiling, grilling, or baking.
5. Additional considerations:
- Be aware of potential parasite infestations in freshwater shellfish and ensure the water source is reasonably free from contamination.
shellfish, bivalves, cooking shellfish, parasites, freshwater shellfish, saltwater mussels
