Fire Building (5) -

Building a Fire Platform in Flooded Areas
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Create a safe platform above floodwater to build a fire.

Materials Needed:
• Four forked stakes (straight, with forks projecting from the side)
• Cross sticks
• Additional sticks for platform construction
• Mud

Steps:
1....
Objective: Create a safe platform above floodwater to build a fire.

Materials Needed:
• Four forked stakes (straight, with forks projecting from the side)
• Cross sticks
• Additional sticks for platform construction
• Mud

Steps:
1. Prepare Stakes: Cut four forked stakes that are long enough so the fork is about 6" to 12" [15 to 30 cm] above water level after being driven into the ground.
2. Drive Stakes: Insert the stakes vertically into the ground, ensuring they are straight and spaced evenly.
3. Add Cross Sticks: Lay two cross sticks across the forks at either end of the platform.
4. Build Platform Base: Place a layer of sticks side by side on top of the cross sticks to form the base of the platform.
5. Cover with Mud: Apply a couple of inches of mud over the sticks to create a stable, waterproof surface for the fire.
6. Construct Fire: Build your fire directly on the mud-covered platform.
7. Extend Platform (Optional): Extend one end of the platform by 1' or 2' [30 or 60 cm] to use as storage for firewood.
fire platform,flooded areas,bushcraft,emergency fire building
Building a Solid Camp Fire (Pyramid Method)
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Construct a long-lasting and efficient campfire using the pyramid method.

Materials Needed:
• Logs (3' to 5' [0.9 to 1.5 m] long)
• Tinder, kindling, and firestarter materials

Steps:
1. Lay Base Logs: Place three or four...
Objective: Construct a long-lasting and efficient campfire using the pyramid method.

Materials Needed:
• Logs (3' to 5' [0.9 to 1.5 m] long)
• Tinder, kindling, and firestarter materials

Steps:
1. Lay Base Logs: Place three or four logs about 3' to 5' [0.9 to 1.5 m] long side by side on the ground.
2. Add Second Layer: Lay another layer of logs across the first, forming a second tier if desired. A third layer can be added for stability.
3. Build Pyramid Structure: Construct a small pyramid on top of the logs using smaller pieces of wood, kindling, and tinder.
4. Light Fire: Light the fire at the top of the pyramid. The starter fire will ignite the logs below with falling embers.
5. Fire Behavior: As the core burns away, the outer logs fall inward, constantly feeding the heart of the fire.
6. Advantages: This type of fire radiates heat evenly in all directions and requires no attention during the night. It also minimizes the risk of sparks spreading and starting a bushfire.
pyramid fire,campfire,bushcraft,efficient fire building
Building a Cone or Pyramid Fire for Wet Wood
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Construct a fire that burns efficiently even with wet wood.

Materials Needed:
• Logs (standing end on)
• Tinder, kindling, and firestarter materials

Steps:
1. Position Logs: Arrange the logs so they are standing end on,...
Objective: Construct a fire that burns efficiently even with wet wood.

Materials Needed:
• Logs (standing end on)
• Tinder, kindling, and firestarter materials

Steps:
1. Position Logs: Arrange the logs so they are standing end on, not lying flat on the ground.
2. Build Pyramid or Cone Shape: Construct a pyramid or cone shape with the logs, ensuring that the center is filled with tinder, kindling, and firestarter materials.
3. Light Fire: Light the fire at the core of the structure.
4. Fire Behavior: As the core burns away, the outer logs will fall inward, constantly feeding the heart of the fire.
5. Advantages: This type of fire radiates heat evenly and can burn well even with wet wood due to its efficient design.
cone fire,wet wood,bushcraft,campfire building
Building a Fire for Warmth in the Wild
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Create a long-lasting fire for warmth when sleeping outdoors.

Materials Needed:
• Dead log (ensure it is not a snake hideout)
• Additional solid log (8" to 9" [20 to 23 cm] thick, 6' to 7' [1.8 to 2.1 m] long)

Steps:
1....
Objective: Create a long-lasting fire for warmth when sleeping outdoors.

Materials Needed:
• Dead log (ensure it is not a snake hideout)
• Additional solid log (8" to 9" [20 to 23 cm] thick, 6' to 7' [1.8 to 2.1 m] long)

Steps:
1. Select Log Site: Choose a site for your fire against a dead log that is not suspected of being a snake hideout.
2. Prepare Secondary Log: Select a solid, fairly long log (8" to 9" [20 to 23 cm] thick and 6' to 7' [1.8 to 2.1 m] long) as the secondary log.
3. Position Logs: Push one end of the secondary log against the dead log where you have built your fire, ensuring it is in contact with the main log.
4. Maintain Fire: As the smaller log burns down during the night, simply push it further against the larger log to keep the fire burning.
5. Alternative Method (Star Layout): Lay logs like a star and push their ends together during the night to maintain the fire.
6. Safety Reminder: Always ensure the fire is completely extinguished in the morning before leaving.
campfire,warmth,sleeping fire,bushcraft,fuel management
Building a Pigsty Fire Correctly
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Construct an effective pigsty fire that burns efficiently and safely.

Materials Needed:
• Heavy logs (for the outer layer)
• Light brushwood (for the inner layer)

Steps:
1. Avoid Common Mistake: Do not build a pigsty...
Objective: Construct an effective pigsty fire that burns efficiently and safely.

Materials Needed:
• Heavy logs (for the outer layer)
• Light brushwood (for the inner layer)

Steps:
1. Avoid Common Mistake: Do not build a pigsty fire with heavy logs on the outside and light brushwood inside, as this will cause the inner wood to burn out quickly without igniting the outer logs properly.
2. Correct Layout: If using the pigsty method, ensure that the top two layers of logs are completely across the top of the structure.
3. Cross-Layer Placement: Lay one layer going in one direction and a second layer crossing it at right angles to create an interlocking pattern.
4. Ignition Process: Light the fire from the top. As the light brushwood burns out, air will flow underneath, allowing heat to be reflected downward and ignite the heavy logs properly.
5. Heat Distribution: This method ensures better radiation of heat than a simple pigsty construction.
pigsty fire,bushcraft,campfire,efficient burning

Trapping And Snaring (5) -

Neutralize Human Scent on Traps Using Stronger Natural Scents
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Identify plants in the bush whose leaves emit a strong perfume when crushed.
2. Crush these leaves thoroughly in your hands before, during, and after setting up your trap or snare.
3. This action will leave the scent of the...
Procedure:

1. Identify plants in the bush whose leaves emit a strong perfume when crushed.
2. Crush these leaves thoroughly in your hands before, during, and after setting up your trap or snare.
3. This action will leave the scent of the crushed leaves on the trap, which is stronger than human scent.
4. The animal will detect the stronger plant scent rather than the human scent, reducing suspicion.

Important Notes:
• Your own scent will still be present in the surrounding area, so this method may not completely eliminate suspicion.
• This technique works best in true bush conditions where animals are wary of humans.
• Avoid using this method with animals that have become accustomed to human presence (e.g., rats, dogs, cats).
scent neutralization,traps,snare,bushcraft,animal trapping
Use Animal Urine as a Lure to Attract Target Species
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Locate and kill a female animal of the species you wish to trap, preferably when she is 'on heat' or 'in season'.
2. Collect her urine directly from the bladder immediately after death.
3. Store the urine in a clean, sealed...
Procedure:

1. Locate and kill a female animal of the species you wish to trap, preferably when she is 'on heat' or 'in season'.
2. Collect her urine directly from the bladder immediately after death.
3. Store the urine in a clean, sealed container for future use.
4. Apply a small amount of the collected urine onto the bait or near the trap/snares to attract male animals of the same species.

Important Notes:
• This method is highly effective and should be used with caution to avoid attracting unwanted attention from other animals.
• Use only fresh urine for optimal results.
• This technique works best in true bush conditions where animals are not accustomed to human presence.
animal urine,lure,traps,snare,bushcraft
Use Essential Oils as Lures to Attract Animals
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Obtain essential oils such as aniseed oil or rhodium oil.
2. Apply a very light touch of the chosen oil onto the bait or near the trap/snares.
3. Ensure that the amount used is minimal, as too much may repel animals instead of...
Procedure:

1. Obtain essential oils such as aniseed oil or rhodium oil.
2. Apply a very light touch of the chosen oil onto the bait or near the trap/snares.
3. Ensure that the amount used is minimal, as too much may repel animals instead of attracting them.
4. Monitor the effectiveness of the lure and adjust the quantity if necessary.

Important Notes:
• These oils are effective in masking human scent and can also act as attractants for certain species.
• Use only high-quality essential oils to avoid contamination or ineffective results.
• This method is particularly useful when trapping animals that are highly sensitive to human scent.
essential oils,lure,traps,snare,bushcraft
Use Fire to Remove Human Scent from Traps
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Collect dry grass or dead leaves and create a small torch.
2. Use the torch to scorch the trap or snare thoroughly.
3. The heat from the fire will destroy human scent on the trap, making it less detectable to animals.
4. Ensure...
Procedure:

1. Collect dry grass or dead leaves and create a small torch.
2. Use the torch to scorch the trap or snare thoroughly.
3. The heat from the fire will destroy human scent on the trap, making it less detectable to animals.
4. Ensure that the trap is completely scorched, not just partially burned.

Important Notes:
• This method is particularly effective in true bush conditions where animals are highly sensitive to human scent.
• Fire should be used carefully to avoid damaging the trap or snare itself.
• This technique may also help mask other scents that could alert animals to the presence of a trap.
fire,traps,snare,bushcraft,scent removal
Use Food Lures to Attract Animals to Traps and Snares
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Select small particles of food that are likely to attract the target animal species.
2. Scatter these food particles lightly around the area where traps or snares will be placed.
3. Ensure that the food is distributed thinly along...
Procedure:

1. Select small particles of food that are likely to attract the target animal species.
2. Scatter these food particles lightly around the area where traps or snares will be placed.
3. Ensure that the food is distributed thinly along an animal trail and more densely near the trap or snare itself.
4. Monitor the area regularly to observe if animals are being attracted by the food lure.

Important Notes:
• Some human scent may remain on the food particles, so it's important to use them in conjunction with other scent-neutralizing techniques.
• Animals that initially show suspicion may become less cautious over time as they find no harm in scavenging around the trap area.
• This method is particularly effective for animals that are drawn to food sources.
food lure,traps,snare,bushcraft,animal attraction

Fishing (5) -

Constructing and Using a Baited Float Stick for Fishing
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Catch fish using a baited float stick in calm water or near beaches with an inshore set.

Materials Needed:
• Stick (2 feet [60 cm] long)
• Heavy stone
• Straps of bark or cane strips
• Fishing line (stout, short length)
• Baited...
Objective: Catch fish using a baited float stick in calm water or near beaches with an inshore set.

Materials Needed:
• Stick (2 feet [60 cm] long)
• Heavy stone
• Straps of bark or cane strips
• Fishing line (stout, short length)
• Baited hook or boomerang-shaped piece of bone/shell

Procedure:
1. Prepare the Float Stick:
- Cut a stick to 2 feet in length.
- Attach a heavy stone to one end using straps made from bark or cane strips, ensuring it is securely bound around the stone.
2. Attach the Fishing Line:
- Tie a stout short length of fishing line to the top end of the stick.
- The line should be 2–3 yards (metres) long.
3. Add Bait:
- Attach a baited hook or a boomerang-shaped piece of bone or shell to the far end of the cord.
4. Deploy the Float Stick:
- Throw the float stick into the water and allow it to drift with the current.
5. Monitor the Drift:
- Observe the direction of the drift or current to know where to look for the sticks after some time.
6. Recover Fish:
- After several hours, retrieve the float stick from the area where it has drifted, and check for any fish that have been caught by the hook or boomerang-shaped bait.

Notes:
• This method is effective in calm water or near beaches with an inshore set.
• Fish are exhausted by struggling against the drag of the float stick, making them easier to recover.
float stick fishing, baited float stick, boomerang-shaped bait, drift fishing, inshore set fishing
Creating a Stick Snare for Surface Feeding Fish
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Catch surface feeding fish using a weighted stick with a noose.

Materials Needed:
• Stick (10–12 inches [25–30 cm] long)
• Small chip of stone
• Gut, horsehair, or thin material for the noose
• Optional: Sticking plaster or...
Objective: Catch surface feeding fish using a weighted stick with a noose.

Materials Needed:
• Stick (10–12 inches [25–30 cm] long)
• Small chip of stone
• Gut, horsehair, or thin material for the noose
• Optional: Sticking plaster or scotch tape to secure the glass bottom to a tin

Procedure:
1. Prepare the Stick:
- Select a stick that is 10–12 inches long.
- Secure a small chip of stone on one side using tying or by splitting the stick and inserting the stone into the split.
2. Attach the Noose:
- Tie a noose made from gut, horsehair, or thin material to the same side as the stone chip.
3. Deploy the Snare:
- Throw multiple weighted sticks with nooses into the sea from a rocky promontory.
4. Fish Behavior and Capture:
- Surface feeding fish like Long Toms and Garfish hide under floating debris for protection against predators.
- These fish will hide under the noose sticks, and their bills or tails may become caught in the noose.
5. Recovery Process:
- After a couple of hours, retrieve the sticks from the beach where they have drifted with any snared fish.
6. Reuse the Snare:
- Recover the sticks and reuse them for future fishing sessions.

Notes:
• This method is effective in areas where surface feeding fish are common.
• Fish become tired after struggling against the noose, allowing the surf to wash them ashore.
stick snare, surface feeding fish, noose stick, fishing with sticks, Long Toms, Garfish
Fishing with a Spear
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Catch fish using a spear in shallow waters.

Materials Needed:
• Fire-hardened stick or barbed trident
• Strong light source (acetylene torch, 5–6 cell electric torch)
• Sea glass or underwater goggles
• Optional: Barbed heavy...
Objective: Catch fish using a spear in shallow waters.

Materials Needed:
• Fire-hardened stick or barbed trident
• Strong light source (acetylene torch, 5–6 cell electric torch)
• Sea glass or underwater goggles
• Optional: Barbed heavy wire

Procedure:
1. Choose the Right Time and Location:
- Fish spearing is most effective over shallow sandy shallows at night.
2. Prepare Your Equipment:
- Use a fire-hardened stick or barbed trident for better efficiency.
- If using wire, ensure it is barbed to increase the chance of catching fish.
3. Use a Light Source:
- Use an acetylene torch or a powerful electric torch to illuminate the water and spot fish.
4. Approach Fish Quietly:
- Move slowly and quietly toward the fish, allowing for the angle of distortion in the water.
5. Pin the Fish with the Spear:
- Position the spear over the fish and jab suddenly when ready to strike.
6. Use Vision Aids:
- Use sea glass (a tin with a hole cut out) or underwater goggles to see clearly without surface ripple interference.
7. Fishing by Day:
- From a boat, raft, coracle, or rocky ledge.
- Spear vertically when fishing from a boat.
8. Fish Identification Tips:
- Fish have natural protective coloring and are hard to see at first.
- They become visible when they move or cast a shadow against the sea bottom.

Notes:
• Fish spearing is more effective in shallow, sandy areas where fish can be pinned down easily.
• Use of barbed wire or multi-pronged spears increases efficiency.
fishing spear, fish spearing, shallow water fishing, barbed trident, sea glass, underwater goggles
Constructing and Using Fish Traps
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
### Constructing and Using Fish Traps

Fish traps can be constructed using natural materials found in the environment or repurposed items. Here is a general procedure for creating and using a fish trap:

1. Choose a Location: Select an area...
### Constructing and Using Fish Traps

Fish traps can be constructed using natural materials found in the environment or repurposed items. Here is a general procedure for creating and using a fish trap:

1. Choose a Location: Select an area where fish are likely to congregate, such as near river bends, shallow waters, or around structures like rocks or logs.
2. Build the Trap: Use materials such as reeds, branches, or woven nets to create a funnel-shaped structure that guides fish into a confined space. The entrance should be wide enough for fish to swim in but narrow enough to prevent them from escaping once inside.
3. Secure the Trap: Anchor the trap firmly to the riverbed or surrounding vegetation to ensure it remains stable and does not float away with the current.
4. Bait the Trap (Optional): Place bait near the entrance of the trap to attract fish into the enclosure.
5. Check Regularly: Inspect the trap periodically to remove any caught fish and prevent them from escaping or suffering due to prolonged entrapment.

Important Notes: Fish traps should be used responsibly and in accordance with local fishing regulations. Overuse or improper placement can harm fish populations and disrupt ecosystems.
fish trap, natural materials, fishing techniques
Setting Up a Blind Roller for an Automatic Fisherman
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
### Setting Up a Blind Roller for an Automatic Fisherman

1. Attach the Blind Roller: Fix a discarded blind roller to a pole or convenient branch of a tree using its bracket.
2. Secure the Fishing Line: Tie the fishing line to the roller and...
### Setting Up a Blind Roller for an Automatic Fisherman

1. Attach the Blind Roller: Fix a discarded blind roller to a pole or convenient branch of a tree using its bracket.
2. Secure the Fishing Line: Tie the fishing line to the roller and pull it so that it touches the water or reaches the desired tension.
3. Engage the Pawls: Ensure the pawls on the roller are engaged before proceeding.
4. Rewind the Roller: Remove the roller from its bracket and manually rewind it, ensuring the line is taut enough to play the fish effectively.
5. Lower the Baited Hook: Lower the baited hook into the water while keeping the pawls engaged. This ensures that when a fish strikes, the pawls will disengage, allowing the roller to release tension and pull the fish toward the surface.
6. Retrieve the Catch: Once the fish is near the surface, unhook it, rebait the line, and cast again for another catch.

Important Notes: It is generally better to set the blind roller on a pole that can be lashed horizontally above the water to a tree or stake. This makes it easier to remove the catch and reset the trap in different locations.
blind roller, automatic fishing, fish trap setup

Fish Farming (5) -

Artificial Breeding of Fish (Fecundation Process)
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Artificial Breeding of Fish

1. Identify Spawning Readiness: Observe the female fish for signs that she is ready to spawn, such as a distended belly and a fluctuation under pressure.

2. Prepare Vessel: Have a flat-bottomed...
Procedure for Artificial Breeding of Fish

1. Identify Spawning Readiness: Observe the female fish for signs that she is ready to spawn, such as a distended belly and a fluctuation under pressure.

2. Prepare Vessel: Have a flat-bottomed vessel containing clear water ready for use.

3. Extract Eggs:
- Use your left hand to hold the female fish by the head and thorax, suspending her over the vessel.
- With your right hand, gently squeeze the loosened eggs through the anal opening into the water.

4. Extract Milt from Male Fish:
- Take a male fish and repeat the squeezing process to extract milt (the white, cream-like substance).
- The milt may flow by simply suspending the fish.

5. Mix Eggs and Milt: Ensure that the eggs and milt are thoroughly mixed in the water. This can be done by gently stirring with your hand or a soft brush.

6. Fecundation Timeframe: Allow the mixture to sit for 2-3 minutes to complete the fecundation process.

Notes:
• The resulting water will resemble whey due to the presence of milt.
• This method is essential for artificial breeding and can be used to transport eggs to other locations for hatching.
artificial breeding, fish fecundation, fish farming, egg extraction, milt collection
Cleaning and Maintenance of Fish Eggs During Hatching
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Cleaning Fish Eggs

1. Monitor Egg Condition: After the fecundation process, observe the eggs in the hatching box.

2. Remove Impurities: If a film or impurity forms on the surface of the eggs due to water contamination,...
Procedure for Cleaning Fish Eggs

1. Monitor Egg Condition: After the fecundation process, observe the eggs in the hatching box.

2. Remove Impurities: If a film or impurity forms on the surface of the eggs due to water contamination, gently clean them using:
- A feather
- A fine brush made from badger's hair

3. Transfer Eggs if Necessary:
- Use a glass pipe with one end closed by your finger to transfer the eggs.
- Remove your finger to allow the eggs to enter the tube and move them to another vessel as needed.

4. Maintain Clean Environment: Ensure that the water remains clean throughout the hatching process to prevent contamination or damage to the eggs.
egg cleaning, fish egg maintenance, water impurity removal
Feeding and Rearing of Young Fish in Artificial Basins
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Feeding and Rearing Young Fish

1. Prepare the Basin: Use a basin with compartments for fish of different ages. The wall should be built waist-high to allow easy observation.

2. Create Natural Conditions:
- Add small...
Procedure for Feeding and Rearing Young Fish

1. Prepare the Basin: Use a basin with compartments for fish of different ages. The wall should be built waist-high to allow easy observation.

2. Create Natural Conditions:
- Add small heaps of rounded pebbles on the gravelly bed.
- Place little shelters made from earthenware around the basin for the fish to hide and rest.
- Include a few aquatic plants to mimic natural environments.

3. Feed Appropriately: Provide food based on the species and age of the fish:
- For salmon, trout, and eel: Use boiled beef or horse-flesh pounded in a mortar.
- After 8-10 days, switch to raw meat, also pounded into small pellets.
- In some cases, use flesh from cheaper fish or small earthworms and crustacea for feeding.

4. Monitor Growth: Fish like pike and trout can be released into ponds or rivers after a short period, while more delicate species should remain in artificial basins until they are strong enough to survive in natural waters.

5. Scale of Rearing: In some cases, up to 2000 salmon can be reared in a box measuring less than 2 feet long, 6 inches wide, and 4 inches deep.
young fish feeding, artificial rearing, fish basin setup
Hatching Apparatus for Fish Eggs
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Setup of Hatching Apparatus

1. Choose a Container: Use a long and narrow wooden box lined with zinc or lead, or an earthenware fish box.

2. Prepare the Frame: Construct a movable frame or grate composed of glass rods spaced about...
Setup of Hatching Apparatus

1. Choose a Container: Use a long and narrow wooden box lined with zinc or lead, or an earthenware fish box.

2. Prepare the Frame: Construct a movable frame or grate composed of glass rods spaced about one-tenth of an inch apart to hold the eggs.

3. Ensure Running Water: The hatching trough must be supplied with running water for optimal conditions. In laboratory settings, troughs made of potter's enamelled ware are used.

4. Place Eggs on Frame: Spread the impregnated eggs onto the glass rod frame inside the container.

5. Maintain Water Flow: Ensure a constant flow of clean water through the apparatus to support hatching.

Notes:
• The use of running water is essential for successful hatching, as it mimics natural conditions.
• Avoid using wire gauze sieves in ponds and rivers due to potential damage to eggs and young fish.
hatching apparatus, fish egg hatching, running water, glass rod frame
Transportation of Fish Eggs and Young Fry
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Transporting Fish Eggs

1. Prepare Packaging Based on Egg Type:
- For eggs with a tough covering (e.g., salmon and trout): Use pine boxes filled with sand, moss, sponge fragments, or aquatic plants to hold the eggs in...
Procedure for Transporting Fish Eggs

1. Prepare Packaging Based on Egg Type:
- For eggs with a tough covering (e.g., salmon and trout): Use pine boxes filled with sand, moss, sponge fragments, or aquatic plants to hold the eggs in layers.
- For agglutinated clusters of eggs (e.g., carp, roach, perch): Place them in jars that are three-quarters full of water and contain some aquatic plant.
- For eggs deposited on grass or small sticks: Wrap the grass or sticks with a wet cloth and place them in a box or basket.

2. Transport Young Fry:
- Use bottles containing water and living aquatic plants to transport young fish.
- Ensure that the water is renewed regularly during transit.

3. Maintain Oxygen Supply: To ensure adequate air supply for the fish, use an apparatus such as a bellows (like those used in Scottish bagpipes) operated under the arm to send air through the water containing the fish. This method was devised by fishermen of the Vosges.
transporting fish eggs, young fry transport, oxygen supply for fish

Tools Required: A sharp ten- or twelve-inch chef's knife, a paring knife, and a boning or fillet knife with a six- to eight-inch-long blade.

Steps:
1. Rinse the fish thoroughly under running water.
2. Place the fish on a flat surface, holding it by the tail.
3. Use the back of a knife, a spoon, or a fish scaler to begin scraping from the tail toward the head with short, authoritative strokes.
4. Scale the entire fish, including the belly and back.
5. Rinse the fish well after scaling.
scale fish, clean fish, fish preparation, fishing skills, bushcraft

Navigation (4) -

Constructing an Improvised Quadrant for Measuring Elevation of Heavenly Bodies
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• A straight stick
• A whippy length of cane or very pliable stick
• A puddle of water (to serve as a horizon)

Steps to Construct the Quadrant:
1. Mark the Straight Stick:
- Mark off the straight stick into 57 equal...
Materials Needed:
• A straight stick
• A whippy length of cane or very pliable stick
• A puddle of water (to serve as a horizon)

Steps to Construct the Quadrant:
1. Mark the Straight Stick:
- Mark off the straight stick into 57 equal divisions. The actual length of each division is not important, but they must be as regular as possible.
2. Mark the Whippy Stick:
- Mark off the whippy cane or pliable stick into 90 equal divisions of the same length as those on the straight stick. For convenience, these can be marked at five or ten division intervals; however, a smaller stick should be marked with separate divisions for accurate reading between these marks.

Using the Quadrant:
1. Positioning the Straight Stick:
- Lay the straight stick across the puddle in line with the heavenly body to be measured (e.g., the sun or stars).
2. Fixing the Whippy Stick:
- Push the end of the 90-division stick into the ground so that the first mark is just level with the water surface.
3. Bending the Whippy Stick:
- Bend the whippy stick by hand until it assumes the shape of a semicircle, ensuring that its other end reaches the other end of the 57-division straight stick. Push this end into the soft dirt beneath the water.

Measuring the Angle of Elevation:
1. Positioning Your Eye:
- Place your eye against one of the divisions on the semicircular whippy stick, on the side farthest from the object to be viewed (e.g., the sun or stars).
2. Observing the Reflection:
- Look at the surface of the water for the reflection of the heavenly body.
3. Aligning the Pointer Stick:
- Move a free hand or pointer stick along the far side of the curve until it cuts off the ray of light from the heavenly body.

Calculating the Angle of Elevation:
1. Count Divisions:
- Count the number of divisions from the water to where the pointer stick cuts off the ray of light (this is the angle of incidence).
- Count the number of divisions from the water to the division against which your eye was placed (this is the angle of reflection).
2. Add the Divisions:
- Add these two numbers together to get the total angle of elevation above the horizon.

Accuracy Tips:
• With careful shaping of the whippy stick and precise measurement of divisions, you should be able to read angles with an accuracy of up to a quarter of a degree.
improvised quadrant,elevation measurement,navigation,bushcraft,heavenly body
Create a Camp Sun Clock
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Pegs
• Cord
• Knife
• Level, open patch of bare earth

Steps to Create a Camp Sun Clock:
1. Select Location: Choose a level, open patch of bare earth near your camp that is exposed to sunlight throughout the day.
2....
Materials Needed:
• Pegs
• Cord
• Knife
• Level, open patch of bare earth

Steps to Create a Camp Sun Clock:
1. Select Location: Choose a level, open patch of bare earth near your camp that is exposed to sunlight throughout the day.
2. Mark Center Point: Insert a peg in the center of this area. Use a length of cord as a loop around the peg and scratch a circle on the ground with a radius of at least 5' [1.5 m].
3. Draw True North Line: Using a compass or other method, draw a line from the central peg that points to True North (not Magnetic North). Extend this line so it intersects the southern edge of the circle.
4. Add East-West Lines: Draw accurate East and West lines crossing at the center of the circle.
5. Divide Circle into 24 Equal Parts: Divide the circumference of the circle into twenty-four equal divisions, each representing fifteen degrees.
6. Determine Latitude: Using your map, find your current latitude in degrees. Mark this on the outside of the circle by placing small pegs at the appropriate degree points, noting whether you are north or south of the equator.
7. Stretch Cord Between Pegs: Stretch a cord over the two pegs marking your latitude and mark where it crosses the North-South line. Insert a peg at this intersection point on the North-South line.
8. Mark East-West Line Ends: Place two additional pegs at either end of the East-West line so that the 'degree' pegs are positioned at right angles to these lines.
9. Tie Cords Around Pegs: Tie a cord to each of the East-West pegs, allowing it to pass around the peg on the North-South line. Lift the cord over the central peg and use your knife to scratch an ellipse on the ground that touches both the circle at the point where the East-West line crosses and the peg on the North-South line.
10. Connect 15-Degree Marks: Use a cord to connect the fifteen-degree marks on the circle, aligning them parallel with the North-South line. Where the cord intersects the ellipse, insert small pegs firmly into the ground.
11. Place Hour Pegs: There will be thirteen pegs following the curve of the ellipse. These are the hour pegs: starting from 6 a.m. on the left (where the West line cuts the circle), continuing to 12 noon at the North-South line, and ending with 6 p.m. on the right (where the East line cuts the circle).

Shadow Stick Placement:
• The position of the shadow stick depends on whether the sun is north or south of the equator. Ensure that your setup accounts for this to maintain accuracy.
camp sun clock, navigation, true north, latitude, shadow stick, ellipse, hour pegs
Create a Sun Compass on a Map or Ground
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure for Creating a Sun Compass/Sun Clock:

1. Choose the Surface:
- On a map: Select an area where important information will not be obliterated.
- On the ground: Ensure the area is clean, level, and open to sunshine throughout...
Procedure for Creating a Sun Compass/Sun Clock:

1. Choose the Surface:
- On a map: Select an area where important information will not be obliterated.
- On the ground: Ensure the area is clean, level, and open to sunshine throughout the day.

2. Draw a Circle:
- Size depends on the surface:
- Map: 6" or 7" (15 cm or 17.5 cm)
- Card: 2" or 3" (5 cm or 7.5 cm)
- Ground: 3' to 6' (0.9 m to 1.8 m)

3. Mark True North Line:
- This is critical for accuracy.
- On a map, use the grid lines or the NORTH arrow head to determine TRUE North (not magnetic).
- Draw a line from North to South through the center of the circle.

4. Draw East-West Line:
- Draw another line through the center of the circle from East to West, perpendicular to the North-South line.

5. Divide Circle into 24 Equal Parts (15° Each):
- Use a protractor if on a card or use a cord for radius measurements if on the ground.
- Divide the outer circumference of the circle into 24 equal divisions, each representing 15 degrees.

6. Mark Divisions with Faint Lines:
- Connect these divisions with faint lines parallel to the North-South line.

7. Determine Latitude (if Unknown):
- Consult your map for approximate latitude within an error of five degrees.
- Start on the East-West line at zero, move up toward North or down toward South along the circle until you can roughly pinpoint your current latitude relative to the Equator.

8. Mark Latitude Point:
- Mark where this point intersects the North-South line.

9. Draw an Ellipse:
- Place two pins (or pegs) on the East-West line, aligned with the latitude points on the circle and parallel to the North-South line.
- Tie a thread or cord between these pins, ensuring it spans from one pin on the East-West line to the corresponding point on the North-South line.
- Insert a pencil (or knife back) into the loop of the thread and draw an ellipse that touches both the latitude point on the North-South line and the East-West intersection point.

10. Mark Hours on Ellipse:
- Start with 12 at the top of the North-South line.
- Mark 6 p.m. on the east side and 6 a.m. on the west side (since the sun in the East casts shadows to the West and vice versa).
- Use Roman numerals for clarity.

11. Add Half and Quarter Hour Intervals:
- Estimate these intervals with reasonable accuracy and mark them on the ellipse.

12. Create a Declination Circle:
- Mark 23½ degrees on the outer circle of the Sun Compass and transfer this to the North-South line.
- Use this as a radius to draw a smaller circle from the center of the main circle.

13. Set Up Shadow Stick (Gnomon):
- Place the shadow stick vertically on the North-South line, opposite the appropriate date on the declination circle.
- Ensure it is vertical using a plumb-bob.

14. Use the Sun Compass as a Clock:
- The shadow will fall across the elliptical line, indicating Solar Time.
- Adjust for Equation of Time and Longitude Corrections to obtain accurate standard time.

Note: A Sun Compass drawn on an 'ordnance' (military) map can be used to obtain accurate time by orienting the map with recognizable ground features and making necessary corrections.
sun compass, sun clock, navigation, latitude, declination circle, shadow stick, gnomon, true north, ellipse, time correction
Measure the Distance Across a River or Gorge
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Stakes or markers
• A known number of paces

Steps to Measure the Distance:
1. Select a mark on the opposite bank ('A') and drive a stake on the near bank ('B').
2. Walk at right angles for a known number of paces,...
Materials Needed:
• Stakes or markers
• A known number of paces

Steps to Measure the Distance:
1. Select a mark on the opposite bank ('A') and drive a stake on the near bank ('B').
2. Walk at right angles for a known number of paces, placing another marker stake ('C'), then continue an equal number of paces and place a third marker ('D').
3. Turn at right angles away from the river and move back until the center marker ('C') and the mark on the opposite bank ('A') are in line with a new point ('E').
4. Measure the distance from the last marker ('D') to the point 'E'. This distance will equal the width of the river or gorge.
measure river, distance across river, survival, navigation

1. Sharpen one end of the stick to a point.
2. Rub the sharpened end fairly smooth on a stone to prevent splitting during use.
3....
Materials needed: A dead, straight-grained stick 1" [2.5 cm] thick and about 18" [45 cm] long.

Steps:
1. Sharpen one end of the stick to a point.
2. Rub the sharpened end fairly smooth on a stone to prevent splitting during use.
3. Narrow the other end until it is about ¼" [6 mm] thick, but keep the full width of the stick intact.
4. Flatten this narrowed end for about 3" [7.5 cm] to create a flat surface for tying.
5. Cut an eye through the flat side, approximately ¼" [6 mm] wide and at least ½" [12 mm] long, about two inches from the end.

Usage:
• Use this needle to sew thatching material securely onto the thatching battens.

Create a Stick Thatch for Secure Coverage

Steps:
1. Tie sticks about 2' [60 cm] apart on the thatching batten.
2. Tie one end of a thatching stick to the batten.

Materials needed: Sticks (for tying), thatching material, and thatching battens.

Steps:
1. Tie sticks about 2' [60 cm] apart on the thatching batten.
2. Tie one end of a thatching stick to the batten.
3. Place the thatching material under the stick and position it so that the free end reaches the next tie point on the batten.
4. When the next tie is reached, secure the other end of the stick to the batten, binding the thatching in place.

Important:
• This method is useful when long lengths of sewing material are not available.
• Follow the same overlapping and general principles as with sewn thatching for maximum effectiveness.

Create a Tuft Thatch for Secure and Neat Coverage
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials needed: Long, pliable thatching material (e.g., reeds or sedges), gloves or socks (optional for protection).

Steps:
1. Gather the thatching material into small sheaves, about 1" [2.5 cm] thick.
2. **Bend the butt end of each...
Materials needed: Long, pliable thatching material (e.g., reeds or sedges), gloves or socks (optional for protection).

Steps:
1. Gather the thatching material into small sheaves, about 1" [2.5 cm] thick.
2. Bend the butt end of each sheaf over the thatching batten and secure it by twisting a few strands around the sheaf and pushing them through the bunched-up material to hold it in place.
3. Slide the tuft along the thatching batten so that it lies alongside the preceding tuft, leaving about ½" [12 mm] or more between the bent-over ends on the battens.
4. Repeat this process, ensuring each new row of tufts overlaps the two or three preceding rows to maintain a secure and waterproof layer.

Important:
• This method is ideal for reeds, sedges, or sword grasses.
• Wear gloves or socks when working with sharp materials like sedges or sword grasses to avoid cuts.
• The open space between bent-over ends will be covered by the free ends of the next row.
tuft thatch,bushcraft,secure thatching,pliable material
Sew Thatching Material to Battens
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials needed: Thatching material (grass, reeds, etc.), thatching needle with eye, and a timber hitch.

Steps:
1. Lay the thatching material with the butts facing upward toward the roof and the lower end on the lowest batten.
2....
Materials needed: Thatching material (grass, reeds, etc.), thatching needle with eye, and a timber hitch.

Steps:
1. Lay the thatching material with the butts facing upward toward the roof and the lower end on the lowest batten.
2. Secure one end of the sewing material using a timber hitch to the thatching batten.
3. Thread the other end through the eye of the thatching needle.
4. Sew in the ordinary manner to secure the thatching material onto the batten.
5. Avoid creating holes where the sewing may cause the thatching to bunch together by passing the needle through the thatch at an angle and pushing the thatch over the crossing of the stitches.

Important:
• Ensure that each layer is held by three rows of stitching for maximum security.
• The stitching of every row must be completely covered by the free ends of the next layer above it to prevent leaks.

Construct a Fireside Camp Seat Using Forked Stakes
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Two short forked stakes
• Two back forked stakes (3½' [1.05 m] long)
• Cross-bars (about 3" [7.5 cm] thick)
• Straight smooth sticks for the seat and backrest

Steps:
1. Drive two short forked stakes into the ground,...
Materials Needed:
• Two short forked stakes
• Two back forked stakes (3½' [1.05 m] long)
• Cross-bars (about 3" [7.5 cm] thick)
• Straight smooth sticks for the seat and backrest

Steps:
1. Drive two short forked stakes into the ground, ensuring that their forks are pointing outward, away from each other.
- The bottom of the forks should be about 8" to 10" [20 to 25 cm] above the ground level.

2. Drive two back forked stakes (each approximately 3½' [1.05 m] long) into the ground, 15" to 18" [38 to 45 cm] behind the short stakes.
- These back stakes should be driven in at a slight angle, leaning away from the two forward forks.
- The forks of the rear stakes should also point outward.

3. Ensure that both the short and long stakes are not less than 2" [5 cm] thick, with the fork at least 1½" [3.5 cm] thick.

4. Cut two cross-bars, each about 3" [7.5 cm] thick. Make nicks in these so they fit snugly into the forks and connect the front and rear forks.

5. Lay straight smooth sticks (1" to 2" [2.5 to 5 cm] thick) lengthways across the seat portion of the chair. These must be close together.

6. Along the backrest, lash similar sticks spaced from 2" to 3" [5 to 7.5 cm] apart.

Placement Tips:
• The short stakes should be placed at a convenient distance from the fireplace, typically between 3' to 6' [0.9 to 1.8 m], depending on the size of your fire.
fireside seat, forked stakes, cross-bars, survival seating
Constructing a Portable Camp Table with Seats
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Required:
• Dead timber (not green wood)
• Forked stakes (at least 3" [7.5 cm] thick and 4' to 5' [1.2 to 1.5 m] long)
• Straight stakes for framework, table top, and seats
• Rope or lashing material
• Pegs for bracing

Steps:
1....
Materials Required:
• Dead timber (not green wood)
• Forked stakes (at least 3" [7.5 cm] thick and 4' to 5' [1.2 to 1.5 m] long)
• Straight stakes for framework, table top, and seats
• Rope or lashing material
• Pegs for bracing

Steps:
1. Select Forked Stakes: Choose two forked stakes at least 3" [7.5 cm] thick and 4' to 5' [1.2 to 1.5 m] long. The length depends on the soil type and how deep you need to drive them into the ground for stability.

2. Prepare Forked Stakes: Sharpen the lower end of each stake, and bevel the head. Drive the first stake well into the earth so that the lowest part of its crotch is 3' [91 cm] above the ground. The prong should point outward from the table.

3. Position Second Stake: Measure off the desired length for your table (4' to 7' [1.2 to 2.1 m]) and drive in the second stake with its prong pointing away from the first stake. Ensure both stakes are driven to the same depth.

4. Attach Side Poles: Cut four strong straight stakes, 4½' to 5' [1.35 to 1.5 m] long and at least 2½" [6.5 cm] thick. Place these with one end in the crotch of the forks, ensuring they are at right angles to the line of the forked stakes. Make scarf cuts where the sticks cross each other so that they nest securely in the crotch.

5. Attach Table Top Poles: About 2' [60 cm] above ground level, attach two strong poles (2" [5 cm] thick) to the side poles using tight lashings. These will support the table top.

6. Attach Seat Poles: At 15" [37 cm] above ground level, attach two very strong poles (3" [7.5 cm] thick and 7' or 8' [2.1 to 2.4 m] long) to the side poles and forked stakes using tight lashings. These serve as bracing for the seat.

7. Construct Table Top: Cut short, straight sticks for the table top. Use eight sticks per 1' [30 cm] of table length. Lace these sticks side by side across the table top poles.

8. Attach Seat Sticks: Cut seat-sticks (at least 3" to 4" [7.5 to 10 cm] thick) 1' [30 cm] longer than the table's length. Use at least three per side, allowing them to lie on the cross poles so that the distance of the seat from the table can be adjusted by pushing or pulling the sticks.

Bracing for Soft Ground:
• If the ground is soft or sandy, add two diagonal braces from the table level of each forked stake to the foot of the other. Lash where they cross.
• Alternatively, use two 5' [1.5 m] forks to brace below the stakes in the ground. Ensure their butts are firmly seated and held with a stout peg driven into the ground.

Finishing Touches:
• A fly (a piece of fabric) can be thrown over the top bar for shade.
• For dry country, dig two trenches 2' to 3' [60 to 90 cm] apart and at least 10" to 12" [25 to 30 cm] deep. Use sods to raise the seat level and replace them when you leave the camp site.
• Weave grass mats over the seat for cleanliness.
camp table, seat construction, bushcraft, outdoor furniture, survival equipment, portable table
Construct a Camp Chair Using Forked Sticks
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Materials Needed:
• Two stout forked sticks, 4' [1.2 m] long and 3" [7.5 cm] thick
• A third forked stick, 4' [1.2 m] in length
• Straight sticks for the seat and backrest

Steps:
1. Select two stout forked sticks that are approximately 4'...
Materials Needed:
• Two stout forked sticks, 4' [1.2 m] long and 3" [7.5 cm] thick
• A third forked stick, 4' [1.2 m] in length
• Straight sticks for the seat and backrest

Steps:
1. Select two stout forked sticks that are approximately 4' [1.2 m] long and 3" [7.5 cm] thick. The forks must be at a wide angle.
- Cut the straighter of the two prongs on each stick to about 9" to 10" [22.5 to 25 cm].
- Cut the other, wider-angled prong on each stick to about 12" to 15" [30 to 38 cm].

2. Cut a third forked stick that is also approximately 4' [1.2 m] in length. This stick should have prongs long enough to hold the previously cut sticks.

3. Across the seat portion of the chair, lash straight sticks about 1" [2.5 cm] thick. These sticks must be close together on the seat portion but can be spaced 2" or 3" [5 or 7.5 cm] apart along the backrest.

Alternative Method Using Hooked Stakes:
• If suitable forked sticks are not available, use two hooked stakes instead.
- The crotch of each hook should be about 8" [20 cm] above the end of the stick.
- The sticks themselves should be approximately 3½' [1.05 m] long.

4. Lay two side poles, each about 5' [1.5 m] long, through the hooked portion of the stakes.

5. Lash the upper ends of the hooked stakes together behind the chair.

6. Use a forked pole that leads from the upper end where the hooked stakes are lashed and lash it again to the two side poles. This forms the framework for your camp chair.
camp chair, forked sticks, hooked stakes, seat construction, survival gear

Foraging (4) -

Avoiding Fungus Growth for Safety
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Avoid all forms of fungus growth.

• The food value of fungi is negligible.
• Unless you know for certain that a particular fungus is safe to eat, do not touch it.
• Fungi contain poisons that affect the nerves by causing paralysis.
• Many...
Avoid all forms of fungus growth.

• The food value of fungi is negligible.
• Unless you know for certain that a particular fungus is safe to eat, do not touch it.
• Fungi contain poisons that affect the nerves by causing paralysis.
• Many are extremely dangerous, and very little is known about them.
• A few fungi, such as the puffball in its early stage of growth (before the ball has dried and become puffy), may be palatable and safe to eat.
• The author's advice: Leave all fungus growth severely alone.
fungus,poisonous plants,avoid fungi,bushcraft foraging
Testing Seeds and Nuts for Safety
High Priority Source: the ten bushcraft books
Some seeds contain deadly poisons, which may not always be detected by the palate.

• A bitter, strongly acid, or burning 'hot' taste is a sign of poisonous contents.
• Any seeds with these tastes should be avoided.
• Tasting seeds will not...
Some seeds contain deadly poisons, which may not always be detected by the palate.

• A bitter, strongly acid, or burning 'hot' taste is a sign of poisonous contents.
• Any seeds with these tastes should be avoided.
• Tasting seeds will not affect you. The poison may be tasted but must not be swallowed.
• When testing seeds for edibility:
- Taste them and spit out the portion if it is unpalatable.
- There will be no ill-effects from this process.

Nuts (which are seeds) should also be tested carefully.

• Many nuts contain hydrocyanic acid poison, which can be detected by the palate and dissolved by boiling or soaking in water for 10 to 12 hours.
• Other nuts, such as candle nuts, may act as violent purgatives but can become harmless after cooking (boiling or baking).
• Unless you know for certain that the nuts are safe to eat, regard them with suspicion and test by first tasting.
• If taste indicates no poison, then eat a small quantity.
• If there are no ill-effects within one hour, the nut is likely safe.
seeds,nuts,toxic plants,bushcraft foraging,testing food safety
Identifying Edible Berries in Arctic Climates
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
In cold climates, most berries are edible, unlike in tropical and sub-tropical areas where they should be regarded as probably poisonous.

• In tropical areas, the colour red is a danger signal. Avoid all red berries.
• This rule does not...
In cold climates, most berries are edible, unlike in tropical and sub-tropical areas where they should be regarded as probably poisonous.

• In tropical areas, the colour red is a danger signal. Avoid all red berries.
• This rule does not apply in colder climates, where almost all red berries are edible.
• Poisons may be present in berries, so always observe this general rule when dealing with unknown berries.
arctic berries,edible berries,cold climate foraging,bushcraft
Preparing Roots and Tubers for Consumption
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Most roots and tubers are safe to eat, but almost all must be boiled or heat treated before they are digestible.

• The common potato is nearly valueless as food unless cooked.
• Yams are not a specific species of plant. The term 'yam'...
Most roots and tubers are safe to eat, but almost all must be boiled or heat treated before they are digestible.

• The common potato is nearly valueless as food unless cooked.
• Yams are not a specific species of plant. The term 'yam' refers to the root of a ground vine, and sweet potatoes are also yams.
• They are a prolific source of food in tropical and sub-tropical regions.
• Many vines produce these tubers, and all such tubers are edible if free from oxalate of lime crystals.

Hydrocyanic poison is water soluble in most yams. The leaf's veining system can be a guiding principle for identifying plants that may have edible tubers:
• If the veins radiate from the point where the leaf joins the stalk, rather than from a main vein, there may be tubers to dig.

Testing for safety:
• Use your senses: sight, smell, and taste.
- If food looks healthy and clean, it may be safe.
- If it smells all right, proceed to taste it.
- If the taste is acceptable, the food is likely safe.

Remember these rules for safety:
• Be careful with nuts and seeds.
• Regard red as a danger signal in tropical areas.
• Avoid fungi.
roots,tubers,cooking roots,bushcraft foraging,edible plants

Beekeeping (4) -

Prevention of Drone Rearing in Bee Colonies
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Preventing Drone Rearing:

1. Use Movable Frames: Utilize hives with movable frames to easily manage drone comb.
2. Remove Drone Comb: Remove all large comb (used for rearing drones) from the main hive and fasten it in...
Procedure for Preventing Drone Rearing:

1. Use Movable Frames: Utilize hives with movable frames to easily manage drone comb.
2. Remove Drone Comb: Remove all large comb (used for rearing drones) from the main hive and fasten it in surplus honey-boxes where it can be used for storing honey.
3. Monitor Hive Activity: Colonies with fertile queens do not build drone comb, so place these colonies in empty hives and provide them with good worker comb already built.
4. Avoid Drone Rearing: Prevent the rearing of drones as they consume significant amounts of honey without contributing to stores.

Notes: It is unnecessary to construct traps for drones; instead, focus on preventing their existence by managing hive combs effectively.
drone rearing prevention, bee colony management, honey conservation, drone comb removal
Replacing Old Queens in Bee Colonies
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Replacing Old Queens:

1. Assess Queen Age: After the second summer, assess the queen's laying capacity as it begins to decline.
2. Replace with Young Queen: Although an old queen may live for two more seasons, it is better...
Procedure for Replacing Old Queens:

1. Assess Queen Age: After the second summer, assess the queen's laying capacity as it begins to decline.
2. Replace with Young Queen: Although an old queen may live for two more seasons, it is better to replace her with a young one for colony prosperity.
3. Use Movable Frames: With movable frames, this replacement can be done easily and efficiently.

Notes: The use of movable frames simplifies the process of replacing queens and maintaining healthy bee colonies.
queen replacement, hive management, bee colony health, queen age assessment
Constructing Movable Frame Bee Hives
Reference Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Constructing Movable Frame Bee Hives:

1. Use frames that allow combs to be built and secured by bees on movable frames. This enables the bee-keeper to examine, remove, or replace combs without harming the bees.
2. **Ensure...
Procedure for Constructing Movable Frame Bee Hives:

1. Use frames that allow combs to be built and secured by bees on movable frames. This enables the bee-keeper to examine, remove, or replace combs without harming the bees.
2. Ensure each frame is designed with a space between them (the 'bee space') so that bees can build combs properly. This principle was introduced by Rev. L. L. Langstroth and is essential for proper hive function.
3. Construct hives in a simple, practical form. The American improved movable comb hive is recommended as it is easy to construct and safe for winter use.
4. Avoid unnecessary ornamentation or features that are not useful to the bees. A good plain, well-painted hive will last a lifetime and meet the needs of the bees.
5. Provide ample room on all hives for boxes to contain surplus honey. Bees naturally store their choice honey as near the top as possible.
6. Use surplus boxes with one glass side when intended for market. This makes honey more salable, and boxes should hold about six pounds of honey.
7. For family use, use larger boxes that can contain 12 to 20 pounds of honey.
8. Do not place surplus boxes on hives until fruit-blossoms are abundant. Early in the spring, bees focus on rearing brood and would not use surplus boxes.
9. Once bees begin storing honey in the surplus boxes, closely monitor them. When full, replace them immediately with empty ones to prevent bees from becoming idle due to lack of space.
beekeeping,movable frames,hive construction,surplus honey boxes,Langstroth hive
Uniting Two or More Bee Colonies
Reference Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure for Uniting Bee Colonies:

1. Examine Hives: As soon as frost cuts short the honey season, examine all hives using movable combs to determine their exact state.
2. Identify Weak and Strong Colonies: Identify colonies with too...
Procedure for Uniting Bee Colonies:

1. Examine Hives: As soon as frost cuts short the honey season, examine all hives using movable combs to determine their exact state.
2. Identify Weak and Strong Colonies: Identify colonies with too little honey and those with fewer bees but sufficient honey.
3. Prepare for Uniting:
- Blow smoke among both colonies to alarm the bees and induce them to fill their sacs with honey.
- Alternatively, sprinkle both colonies with sugar and water to achieve the same effect.
4. Remove Combs: In a few moments, remove combs from both hives.
5. Arrange Combs: Place full combs in one hive and reserve empty ones for future use.
6. Combine Bees: Brush bees from both colonies together at the entrance of the hive so they can enter peaceably.
7. Remove One Queen: One queen will be destroyed during this process; if possible, choose which queen to remove and kill the other.

Notes: This method ensures that all bees are saved and valuable colonies are formed without loss.
uniting bee colonies, hive management, beekeeping techniques, honey storage, queen removal

Basic Mountaineer Qualification and Responsibilities
Standard Source: FM 3 97 61 Military Mountaineering

The basic mountaineer should be a graduate of a basic mountaineering course and have the fundamental travel and climbing skills necessary to move safely and efficiently in mountainous terrain. These soldiers should be comfortable functioning in this environment and, under the supervision of qualified mountain leaders or assault climbers, can assist in the rigging and use of all basic rope installations.

On technically difficult terrain, the basic mountaineer should be capable of performing duties as the “follower” or “second” on a roped climbing team, and should be well trained in using all basic rope systems. These soldiers may provide limited assistance to soldiers unskilled in mountaineering techniques.

Particularly adept soldiers may be selected as members of special purpose teams led and supervised by advanced mountaineers.

Rope Cord Making (3) -

Identify Suitable Materials for Rope or Cord Making
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Find fibrous materials suitable for making ropes or cords.

• Common sources include:
- Vines
- Grasses

-...
Objective: Find fibrous materials suitable for making ropes or cords.

Materials to Look For:
• Any fibrous material with reasonable length, moderate strength, and flexibility.
• Common sources include:
- Vines
- Grasses
- Barks
- Palms
- Animal hair

Selection Criteria:
• The material should be at least 12" to 24" [30 cm to 60 cm] in length.
• It must have sufficient strength and flexibility for the intended purpose.
• Avoid brittle or overly dry materials unless they are known to be strong.

Testing:
• Always test the rope or cord for its breaking strain before using it for critical tasks like lashing poles or securing shelters.
rope making,cord making,fibrous materials,bushcraft,material selection
Spin or Plait Fibres into Ropes or Cords
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Create a rope or cord by spinning or plaiting fibres.

Steps to Make a Rope or Cord:
1. Select and Prepare the Material: Choose suitable fibrous material as outlined in the previous procedure.
2. **Separate Fibres (if...
Objective: Create a rope or cord by spinning or plaiting fibres.

Steps to Make a Rope or Cord:
1. Select and Prepare the Material: Choose suitable fibrous material as outlined in the previous procedure.
2. Separate Fibres (if necessary): If using materials like bark or grass, separate individual strands for easier spinning.
3. Spin the Fibres:
- Hold a small bundle of fibres between your fingers and twist them together to form a single strand.
- Continue adding more fibres as you spin, maintaining even tension.
4. Plait or Braid (Optional): If making a thicker rope, braid multiple strands together using a simple overhand or three-strand braiding technique.
5. Test the Rope:
- Apply gentle tension to check for strength and durability.
- For critical tasks, test the rope by gradually increasing weight until it breaks (for safety purposes only).

Notes:
• The method used is similar to that of modern machines but done manually.
• This skill is one of the oldest human techniques and can be applied in various survival scenarios.
spinning rope,plaiting fibres,bushcraft techniques,manual rope making
Understand Rope Breaking Strains for Bush-Made Ropes
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Understand the breaking strain of handmade ropes made from different materials.

Breaking Strain by Material (for a 1" [2.5 cm] diameter rope):
• Green Grass: 100 lbs. to 250 lbs. [45 kg to 115 kg]
• Bark Fibre: 500 lbs....
Objective: Understand the breaking strain of handmade ropes made from different materials.

Breaking Strain by Material (for a 1" [2.5 cm] diameter rope):
• Green Grass: 100 lbs. to 250 lbs. [45 kg to 115 kg]
• Bark Fibre: 500 lbs. to 1,500 lbs. [225 kg to 680 kg]
• Palm Fibre: 650 lbs. to 2,000 lbs. [295 kg to 905 kg]
• Sedges: 2,000 lbs. to 2,500 lbs. [905 kg to 1,135 kg]
• Monkey Rope (Lianas): 560 lbs. to 700 lbs. [245 kg to 320 kg]
• Lawyer Vine (Calamus) - ½" [1.25 cm] diameter: 1,200 lbs. [545 kg]

Important Notes:
• Always use the lowest breaking strain value for safety unless you have tested the rope yourself.
• The breaking strain increases with the square of the diameter (e.g., doubling the diameter quadruples the strength).
• Never assume a rope's strength without testing it, especially in life-critical situations.
rope breaking strain,bushcraft rope strength,material strength testing

Animal Senses (3) -

Test Temperature Sensitivity in Leeches
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Demonstrate how leeches use temperature sensitivity to locate food.

Procedure:
1. Gather Materials: Obtain a leech, a screw-top glass jar, and thick paper.
2. Prepare the Jar: Place the leech inside the glass jar and...
Objective: Demonstrate how leeches use temperature sensitivity to locate food.

Procedure:
1. Gather Materials: Obtain a leech, a screw-top glass jar, and thick paper.
2. Prepare the Jar: Place the leech inside the glass jar and cover the sides with thick paper to block out light and sound.
3. Observe Initial Behavior: Note that the leech will stand on its tail in the center of the jar, weaving its upper end around.
4. Introduce Heat Source: Hold a burning stick approximately 1' (30 cm) from one side of the jar.
5. Watch for Response: The leech will begin to loop toward the heat source, even though it cannot see, smell, or hear it.
6. Test Directional Sensitivity: Move the burning stick to the other side of the jar and observe how the leech immediately changes direction toward the new heat source.
7. Understand Purpose: Recognize that this sensitivity helps leeches locate warm-blooded animals for feeding.
temperature sensitivity, leech behavior, animal senses, survival, bushcraft
Test Vibration Sensitivity in Fish
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Demonstrate how fish use vibration sensitivity to detect and respond to movement in water.

Procedure:
1. Gather Materials: Obtain a container of still water, fine sand, small fish, and a heavy stone.
2. **Introduce Fine...
Objective: Demonstrate how fish use vibration sensitivity to detect and respond to movement in water.

Procedure:
1. Gather Materials: Obtain a container of still water, fine sand, small fish, and a heavy stone.
2. Introduce Fine Sand: Throw a small amount of fine sand into the still water.
3. Observe Fish Response: Note that small fish will instantly dart toward the sand due to the vibrations caused by its entry into the water.
4. Test with Heavy Stone: Drop a heavy stone into the water and observe how the fish scatter in response to the strong vibration from the impact.
5. Use Thread and Stone for Controlled Vibration: Tie a thread to a small stone, lower it beneath the water, and pull it taut. Rub the thread lightly to create vibrations in the water.
6. Watch Fish Behavior: Observe how the fish are attracted toward the source of vibration.
7. Cease Vibrations: Stop rubbing the thread and note that the fish disperse again as the vibrations stop.
8. Repeat Test: Continue rubbing the thread to see how the fish are drawn back toward the vibrating area.
9. Understand Purpose: Recognize that this sensitivity helps fish detect prey or danger through water vibrations.
vibration sensitivity, fish behavior, animal senses, survival, bushcraft

Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Objective: Recognize the range of sensory capabilities in animals beyond human perception.

Procedure:
1. Study the Five Human Senses: Understand how sight, sound, touch, taste, and smell function in humans as tools for survival (finding...
Objective: Recognize the range of sensory capabilities in animals beyond human perception.

Procedure:
1. Study the Five Human Senses: Understand how sight, sound, touch, taste, and smell function in humans as tools for survival (finding food, avoiding danger).
2. Explore Additional Sensory Capabilities in Animals: Learn about non-human sensitivities such as:
- Light sensitivity
- Sound sensitivity
- Taste sensitivity
- Touch sensitivity
- Scent sensitivity
- Temperature sensitivity
- Vibration sensitivity
- Supersonic sensitivity
- Telepathetic sensitivity (theorized)
- Directional sensitivity (theorized)
3. Observe Animal Behavior: Note how animals use these sensitivities to navigate, find food, and avoid danger.
4. Compare with Human Capabilities: Understand that while humans have highly developed sight, many animals may excel in other areas such as temperature or vibration sensitivity.
5. Be Cautious with Theoretical Sensitivities: Recognize that some listed sensitivities (e.g., telepathy) are not scientifically proven and should be approached with skepticism.
sensory capabilities, animal senses, survival, bushcraft, temperature sensitivity, vibration sensitivity

Animal Behavior (3) -

Detecting Bat Activity Using Supersonic Sensitivity
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Choose a time and location: Select a summer evening at dusk when bats are likely to be active.
2. Gather materials: Find a small pebble or stone that can be thrown easily without causing damage.
3. Throw the pebble:...
Procedure:

1. Choose a time and location: Select a summer evening at dusk when bats are likely to be active.
2. Gather materials: Find a small pebble or stone that can be thrown easily without causing damage.
3. Throw the pebble: Launch the pebble high into the air, ensuring it is visible in the sky.
4. Observe for bat activity: Watch closely for any sudden movement of bats toward the pebble. You may hear a 'chirp' sound, which is the bat emitting high-pitched sound waves.
5. Watch the bat's behavior: The bat will use its supersonic sensitivity to detect the echo from the pebble and change direction accordingly. This demonstrates how bats navigate using echolocation.
6. Note the bat’s avoidance behavior: If the echo becomes too strong, the bat may avoid the pebble to prevent a collision.

Details:
• Bats use this supersonic sensitivity to locate prey such as insects in flight.
• This ability allows them to navigate through complex environments like mazes of crossed wires without colliding.
• Fruit-eating bats (flying foxes) rely more on acute sense of smell than echolocation.
bat,echolocation,supersonic sensitivity,detecting bat activity
Observing Group Sensitivity in Bird Flight
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Choose a location: Find an open area where birds, particularly pigeons, are likely to be flying.
2. Observe the flock: Watch a group of birds as they fly together in formation.
3. Watch for sudden changes in direction:...
Procedure:

1. Choose a location: Find an open area where birds, particularly pigeons, are likely to be flying.
2. Observe the flock: Watch a group of birds as they fly together in formation.
3. Watch for sudden changes in direction: Note when the entire flock turns or changes course simultaneously without any single bird leading the change.
4. Record observations: Document how all birds respond to the same stimulus at once, indicating a shared 'group sensitivity'.

Details:
• This behavior is not led by one individual but occurs collectively among all members of the group.
• The only plausible explanation for this synchronized movement is that the birds share a form of 'group sensitivity' or collective awareness.
• This ability may be crucial for survival, allowing groups to react quickly to threats or changes in environment.
bird flight,group sensitivity,pigeons
Understanding Directional Sensitivity in Animals and Humans
Reference Source: the ten bushcraft books
Procedure:

1. Observe animal behavior: Study how animals such as birds, fish, and mammals demonstrate directional sensitivity.
2. Watch bird migration or homing behavior: Observe how certain species of birds return to the same nesting...
Procedure:

1. Observe animal behavior: Study how animals such as birds, fish, and mammals demonstrate directional sensitivity.
2. Watch bird migration or homing behavior: Observe how certain species of birds return to the same nesting areas year after year, even over long distances.
3. Study fish breeding habits: Note how some fish species return to their birthplace rivers to spawn, showing a strong sense of direction.
4. Observe human directional sensitivity: Watch individuals who have been trained in navigation or orientation (e.g., hikers, sailors) and note their ability to find their way without external aids.
5. Record observations: Document how these animals and humans use their directional sensitivity for survival and navigation.

Details:
• Directional sensitivity is found across many species of animals and some humans.
• Birds demonstrate this in their migratory patterns, which are often guided by an innate sense of direction.
• Fish show it through their ability to return to the same rivers where they were born for breeding.
• Humans can develop a strong sense of direction through training or experience.
directional sensitivity,animal navigation,bird migration

Animal Baiting (3) -

Conducting a Test Baiting Survey
Standard Source: the ten bushcraft books
Conducting a Test Baiting Survey

Test baiting an area will show what animals are in the locality and what baits they will take.

1. Select a site: Choose an area with light, dusty, clean soil that will clearly show all tracks. The area...
Conducting a Test Baiting Survey

Test baiting an area will show what animals are in the locality and what baits they will take.

1. Select a site: Choose an area with light, dusty, clean soil that will clearly show all tracks. The area should be 3 or 4 yards [metres] square.
2. Drive stakes: Drive ten or a dozen short stakes (each about 1' [30 cm] long) into the ground. Ensure there is at least 3' [90 cm] between each stake.
3. Tie baits to stakes: Tie different baits, some with lures and some without, to the stakes. Use a variety of baits such as seeds, roots, flesh, fruits, or herbage.
4. Make notes: Create a sketch map of the position of the stakes and note what bait each stake carries.
5. Conduct in the afternoon: Perform this work in the afternoon so that animals can access the baits during the night.
6. Check the next morning: Visit the area the following morning to examine the tracks on the soft dust, which will indicate which animals visited and what baits they took.
7. Analyze results: Observe bird tracks at seeds, tree climbing animal tracks around fruit baits, digging claw tracks around root baits, and note which baits were untouched or taken.
8. Use salt as an additive: Salt is a good addition to all baits to enhance their attractiveness.
test baiting,animal survey,bushcraft

Selecting Appropriate Baits for Earth Digging Animals

Earth digging and feeding animals require roots or insects as baits.

• Use roots such as sweet potato, carrot, parsnip, dandelion root, yam, or other ground tubers.
• Insects can also...
Selecting Appropriate Baits for Earth Digging Animals

Earth digging and feeding animals require roots or insects as baits.

• Use roots such as sweet potato, carrot, parsnip, dandelion root, yam, or other ground tubers.
• Insects can also be used to attract burrowing animals.
• Ensure the bait is placed in a location that is accessible for digging.
earth digging animals,bait selection,roots,insects,bushcraft
